Debate on Sodomy in Paradise (Inter-Madhhab Dispute)
Theological debate on Paradise pleasures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that sodomy is intrinsically prohibited and therefore impossible in Paradise, and evaluate the Mu'tazili counterargument based on harm removal.

Core Idea
Sodomy is a defect (ʿāha), not merely a harm; Paradise has no defects, so it cannot occur there.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq records a theological debate on whether sodomy could be a pleasure in Paradise. The debate hinges on whether the prohibition is due to intrinsic ugliness or extrinsic harm. The Hanafi scholar Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini argues forcefully that the act is inherently repugnant, not merely restricted because of worldly consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini claims about sodomy in Paradise.
He says it is a defect (ʿāha) and intrinsically ugly; Paradise is free from defects.
2
Identify the opponent and their argument
Ibn al-Walid al-Mu'tazili argues that sodomy could be permissible in Paradise because the worldly harms (cutting progeny, harm) are absent.
He uses an analogy with wine, which is forbidden in this world due to intoxication but permitted in Paradise.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Trace Ibn al-Walid's reasoning: since the prohibition is harm-based, removing the harm allows the act.
Wine analogy: intoxication is a harm; in Paradise wine does not intoxicate, so it is allowed.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Yusuf distinguishes between acts prohibited due to harm (like wine) and acts prohibited due to intrinsic ugliness (like sodomy).
He argues that the male organ was not created for intercourse, so the act is a defect, and defects have no place in Paradise.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is theological: the Hanafi view affirms the perfection of Paradise, while the Mu'tazili view could lead to weakening the concept of intrinsic evil.
This debate does not affect fiqh rulings in this world; sodomy remains haram by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وهل تكون اللواطة في الجنة الخ) قال السيوطي قال ابن عقيل الحنبلي : جرت مسألة بين أبي علي بن الوليد المعتزلي وبين أبي يوسف القزويني في إباحة جماع الولدان في الجنة فقال ابن الوليد لا يمنع أن يجعل ذلك من جملة اللذات في الجنة لزوال : المفسدة لأنه إنما منع في الدنيا لما فيه من قطع النسل وكونه محلاً للأذى وليس في الجنة ذلك ولهذا أبيح شرب الخمر لما ليس فيه من السكر وغاية العربدة وزوال العقل فذلك لم يمنع من فقال أبو يوسف: الميل إلى الذكور عاهة وهو قبيح في نفسه لأنه محل لم يخلق للوطء ولهذا لم يبح في شريعة بخلاف الخمر وهو مخرج الحدث والجنة نزهت عن العاهات. فقال ابن الوليد العاهة . التلويث بالأذى وإذا لم يبق إلا مجرد الالتذاذ. اهـ. كلامه؛ كذا في حواشي المنح للرملي
His saying: And is there sodomy in Paradise? Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali said: A question occurred between Abu Ali ibn al-Walid al-Mu'tazili and Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini regarding the permissibility of intercourse with male youths in Paradise. Ibn al-Walid said: There is nothing preventing that from being made among the pleasures in Paradise, because the harm is removed; it is only forbidden in this world due to the cutting of progeny and it being a place of harm, and there is no such thing in Paradise. For this reason, drinking wine is permitted [in Paradise] because there is in it no intoxication, extreme fidgeting, and loss of intellect; that did not prevent... So Abu Yusuf said: Inclination to males is a defect (ʿāha), and it is intrinsically ugly because it is an organ not created for intercourse; for this reason it was not permitted in any law, unlike wine, and it removes impurity, and Paradise is purified of defects. Ibn al-Walid said: The defect is contamination with harm; and when nothing remains except mere pleasure... End of his words. As in Hawashi al-Minh al-Ramli.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This is a super-commentary (hashiya) inserted by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, quoting al-Suyuti who reports a debate between the Mu'tazili scholar Ibn al-Walid and the Hanafi scholar Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini (not to be confused with Imam Abu Yusuf). The issue is whether sodomy (specifically homosexual intercourse) could be permissible in Paradise as a form of pleasure. Ibn al-Walid argued that the prohibition in this world is due to harms like cutting off progeny and causing physical harm; these reasons do not apply in Paradise, so it could be permitted there. He analogized to wine, which is forbidden in this world due to intoxication but is permitted in Paradise without that harm. Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini countered that the inclination to males is itself a defect (ʿāha) and an intrinsically ugly act because the male organ is not created for that purpose. Hence it was never permitted in any revealed law. Paradise is free from all defects and ugliness, so such an act cannot occur there. The commentator al-Ramli in his Hawashi al-Minh recorded this. The passage concludes by favoring Abu Yusuf's view: 'والجنة نزهت عن العاهات' (Paradise is purified from defects).

Key Terms
العاهة (al-ʿāhah): Defect or blemish, something that is inherently repugnant and not befitting Paradise.
الولدان (al-wildān): Male youths/servants in Paradise (mentioned in Quran 56:17, 76:19) who serve the believers.
شرب الخمر في الجنة (shurb al-khamr fī al-jannah): Drinking wine in Paradise, which is permitted unlike this world, used as an analogy by Ibn al-Walid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of Abu Yusuf al-Qazwini against the permissibility of sodomy in Paradise?
A) It would cut off progeny, which cannot happen in Paradise
B) It is a defect (ʿāha) and intrinsically ugly, and Paradise is free from defects
C) It would cause harm to the youths, and Paradise has no harm
D) There is no explicit text permitting it in Paradise

Key Takeaway
Paradise is free from all defects; thus, acts considered intrinsically ugly in Islamic law cannot occur there, regardless of the removal of worldly harms.

Ta'zir for Insulting Terms: Conditions and Specific Words
Ruling-with-proof on ta'zir for insults with da'wa distinction and specific terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between da'wa (legal claim) and sabb (insult) for ta'zir, and state the Hanafi ruling for specific insulting terms, including the intra-madhhab dispute on 'O Loti'.

Core Idea
Insults incur ta'zir unless they are judicial claims of theft; but a false claim of zina always triggers punishment.

In Hanafi criminal law, offensive speech is punished by ta'zir (discretionary punishment), not hadd, unless it is an explicit accusation of zina. However, a critical distinction exists between an insult (sabb) and a judicial claim (da'wa). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that a false claim of theft does not lead to ta'zir because the intent is to recover property, while a false claim of zina always does. It then lists various insulting terms—such as 'O hypocrite', 'O fasiq', 'O Loti'—and specifies the rulings, including an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether 'O Loti' warrants hadd or ta'zir.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish da'wa from sabb
Understand that ta'zir applies to insults (sabb), not to judicial claims (da'wa) made in court, except for claims of zina which are always punished if unproven.
2
Apply the qiyas between theft and zina claims
The passage uses qiyas jali: for theft, the claimant can prove ownership without accusing theft; for zina, the only way to prove the claim is by accusing zina, so the accuser is punished even in a judicial setting.
The illa is the impossibility of proving the claim without attributing the crime to the defendant.
3
Identify rulings for specific terms
For terms like 'O hypocrite', 'O disbeliever', 'O Jew', 'O fasiq', 'O fajir', the ruling is ta'zir. Note that some terms (e.g., 'O ruspid') are explicit for zina and require hadd.
Terms may be interpreted by custom; 'O qahba' in some contexts may become qadhf but the relied-upon position is ta'zir.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'O Loti'
Abu Hanifa holds ta'zir because the term is ambiguous; Sahibayn hold hadd because they consider it an accusation of liwat. The mufta bihi is ta'zir, especially when said in anger.
Source: Fath al-Qadir confirms ta'zir is correct.
5
Recognize the role of custom and intention
For terms like 'dayyuth' and 'qurtaban', custom determines whether they are insulting. Intention matters for 'O Loti' but is not asked in absolute ruling; for 'O qahba', the custom may elevate it to qadhf, but the passage upholds ta'zir.

الديوث في عرف مصر. وأشار بقوله يابن القحبة إلى مسألتين: إحداهما إذا شتم أصله فإنه يعزر بطلب الولد كقوله يابن الفاسق يابن الكافر أو النصراني وأبوه ليس كذلك. ثانيهما أنه لو قال لامرأته يا قحبة يعزر ولا يحد للقذف بخلاف يا روسبي فإنه قذف يحد به ؛ كذا في الخانية. لأنه بعقد الذمة معه أن لا نؤذيه .اهـ. قلت ويؤيد كلام المؤلف قول الفتح المار آنفاً لو شتم ذمياً يعزر لأنه ارتكب معصية. قوله : (لو قال لامرأته يا قحبة (الخ قال شارح الوقاية : قيل القحبة من تكون همته الزنا فلا يجد أقول : القحبة أفحش من الزانية لأن الزانية قد تفعل سراً وتأنف منه والقحبة تجاهر به بالأجرة اهـ. قال بعض أصحاب الحواشي : قوله القحبة من تجاهر به بالأجرة يعني فينبغي أن يجب الحد لمن قذف بها، يؤيده قول الظهيرية القحبة الزانية والإنصاف أن يجب الحد في ديارنا إذ لا يستعمله أحد إلا في الزانية سيما حالة الغضب فكأنه صار حقيقة عرفية. وقول الشارح القحبة في العرف أفحش من الزاني لا يخلو من الإشارة إلى هذا المعنى اهـ . قلت : وقد أجاب عن ذلك منلا خسرو في شرحه حيث قال : اللهم إلا أن يقال إن الحد إنما يجب إذا قذف بصريح الزنا أو بما في حكمه بأن يدل عليه اللفظ اقتضاء كما إذا قال لست لأبيك أو لست بابن فلان أبيه في الغضب كما مر، ولفظ القحبة لم يوضع لمعنى الزانية بل استعمل فيه بعد وضعه لمعنى آخر كما . ولا يدل عليه اقتضاء أيضاً مر وهو ظاهر ويؤيده ما قال الزيلعي : لا يقال كيف يجب الحد بقوله لغيره لست لأبيك وهو ليس بصريح في الزنا لاحتمال أن يكون من غيره بالوطء بشبهة لأنا نقول : فيه نسبة له إلى الزنا اقتضاء
That preoccupation with fiqh does not accept his testimony, and the author limited the issues of insult to calling (by name) but it is not a restriction because informing is similar, as when he says 'You are a fasiq' or 'So-and-so is a fasiq' and the like. It is stated in al-Quniya: If he says to him 'O hypocrite' or 'You are a hypocrite,' he is ta'zir-ed. This is if it does not take the form of a claim. It is stated in al-Quniya: If a man claims before a judge a theft and is unable to prove it, he is not ta'zir-ed, unlike a claim of zina, because the intent of a theft claim is to prove property, not to attribute theft to him, unlike a claim of zina, even if he intends to establish the hadd punishment, but he cannot prove it except by attributing zina to him, so he is intending to attribute zina to him. In property, he can prove it without attributing theft to him, so he was not intending to attribute theft to him. And in al-Zahiriyya from Muhammad concerning a man who said, 'If I commit zina, my slave is free,' and the slave claimed that he committed zina, the master is made to swear by Allah that he did not commit zina; if he swears, the slave is not freed and the hadd is obligated against the slave for the master; if he does not swear, the slave is freed and there is no hadd on the one who slandered him, by istihsan. And in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: If a person claims against another a claim that necessitates his disbelief and the claimant is unable to prove what he claimed, nothing is required of him if the statement is made in the manner of a claim before a legitimate judge; but if it is made in the manner of insult or belittlement, he is ta'zir-ed appropriately. The restriction to 'Muslim' in his statement 'or a Muslim' in issues of insult is by agreement, for if a Muslim insults a dhimmi, he commits a sin; as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Quniya, from the chapter on istihlal and returning wrongs: He is ta'zir-ed if he says to a Jew or a Magian 'O disbeliever'; he sins if it pains him. Its implication is that he is ta'zir-ed for committing what causes sin, and he has made the author's words from insult: 'O disbeliever,' 'O hypocrite.' And in al-Muhit: He made from it 'O Jew,' and its apparent meaning is that the insulter does not thereby become a disbeliever. And it is explicitly stated in al-Khulasa: This testimony, etc., mentioned there. And it is not hidden that their acknowledgment of false testimony necessitates ta'zir. His statement: (This is if it does not take the form of a claim.) al-Ramli said: The reference, if it goes back to all that is mentioned in the matn, which is apparent, then it is problematic because of what he mentioned regarding the difference between the claim of theft and zina; reflect on this speech and be insightful upon it. The owner of al-Nahr and Sharh Tanwir al-Absar followed him in it, and Allah is the Giver of success. His statement: (He said) in al-Quniya: 'And if a man claims, etc.' al-Ramli said: The speech of al-Quniya is specific to mentioning theft and zina, and does not address other than them, and you know that the mentioned difference attaches other than theft to zina, since he cannot prove it except by attributing it to him, like zina. And I say: What is mentioned of the difference necessitates the opposite of the mentioned ruling, because for property, when it can be proved without attributing it to theft, by claiming it he apparently intends to attribute it to theft, otherwise he would have turned from it to a property claim, unlike that which cannot be proved except by attributing it to what is its means, because he has no alternative, so he is not apparently intending to attribute it to him; reflect. And this occurred to me before I saw it. The difference appears from another angle: the text in zina that if he does not bring four witnesses he is flogged. His statement: (And its implication is that he is ta'zir-ed.) In al-Nahr: There is reflection in it, and what guides to it will come. In al-Durr al-Mukhtar: Perhaps its face is what passed in 'O fasiq'; reflect. That is, from that he brought shame upon himself before the speaker said it. Some virtuous person said: His statement 'reflect' indicates the weakness of this face, because even if he brought shame upon himself, we have committed that if he responds with 'Labbaik' he disbelieves. And it is not hidden that his statement 'O Rafidi' is equivalent to 'O disbeliever' or 'O innovator,' so he is ta'zir-ed, because the Rafidi is a disbeliever if he insults the two shaykhs, and an innovator if he prefers Ali over them without insult, as in al-Khulasa, and it will come in the chapter of apostasy, Allah willing. And His joining 'O fajir' to 'O fasiq' benefits the difference between them; thus in al-Quniya: If a claimant of insult brings two witnesses, one testifying that he said to him 'O fasiq' and the other that he said to him 'O fajir,' that testimony is not accepted. And he is absolute in his statement 'O loti,' so it benefits that he is not asked about his intention and that he is ta'zir-ed absolutely. And in Fath al-Qadir: And it is said concerning 'O loti' that he is asked about his intention; if he meant that he is from the people of Lot, nothing is upon him; if he meant that he does their deed, he is ta'zir-ed according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is subject to hadd, and the correct is that he is ta'zir-ed if in anger – I say – or in jest from one accustomed to jest and ugly speech. And he mentioned among the terms 'dayyuth' and 'qurtaban.' In al-Mughrib: al-Dayyuth is one who has no gheera (protective jealousy) regarding one who enters upon his wife. And al-Qurtaban is an ugly description of a man who has no gheera, from al-Layth and from al-Azhari: This is from the speech of the town dwellers, and I have not seen the Bedouins utter it nor recognize it. And from it is what is regarding the slander of genera, like shahhat (miser). And the commentator mentioned that al-qurtaban is he who sees with his wife or his female relative a man and leaves him alone with her. And it is said: He is the one who causes gathering between two for a non-praiseworthy purpose. And it is said: He is the one who sends his wife with a mature boy or with his sharecropper to the farm or permits them to enter upon her in his absence. On this, he is ta'zir-ed by the word 'mu'arris' (pimp) and 'dayyuth' in the custom of Egypt. And His statement 'O son of a prostitute' refers to two issues: First, if he insults his parent, he is ta'zir-ed by request of the child, like his saying 'O son of a fasiq,' 'O son of a disbeliever,' or 'Christian,' while his father is not like that. Second, that if he says to his wife 'O prostitute,' he is ta'zir-ed and not subject to hadd for slander, unlike 'O ruspide' (filthy one), which is slander for which hadd is obliged; as in al-Khaniyya. Because by the contract of dhimma, we have committed to not harm him. I say: And the author's speech is supported by the statement of al-Fath previously: If he insults a dhimmi, he is ta'zir-ed because he committed a sin. His statement: (If he says to his wife 'O prostitute,' etc.) The commentator of al-Wiqaya said: It is said that al-qahba is one whose ambition is zina, but I say: al-qahba is more obscene than a zaniya, because the zaniya may do it secretly and be ashamed, while al-qahba does it openly for wages. Some hashw writers said: His statement 'al-qahba is one who does it openly for wages' means that the hadd should be obligatory upon one who slanders with it, supported by the statement of al-Zahiriyya: al-qahba is a zaniya. And fairness is that hadd should be obligatory in our lands, since no one uses it except for a zaniya, especially in anger, so it has become a customary reality. And the commentator's statement 'al-qahba in custom is more obscene than the zaniya' is not devoid of indication to this meaning. I say: And Mulla Khusru answered this in his commentary where he said: However, it can be said that hadd is only obligatory if he slanders with explicit zina or what is in its ruling by requiring the word to indicate it by implication, as when he says 'You are not from your father' or 'You are not the son of so-and-so's father' in anger, as passed. And the word 'qahba' was not put for the meaning of a zaniya, but was used for it after being put for another meaning, and it does not indicate it by implication either, as passed, and it is apparent. And it is supported by what al-Zayla'i said: It is not said how hadd is obligatory by his saying to another 'You are not from your father' when it is not explicit in zina, because of the possibility that it is from someone else by dubious intercourse; for we say: In it there is an imputation of zina by implication.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling-with-proof on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for insulting terms, from the Hanafi text al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It begins by establishing that insults (sabb) incur ta'zir unless they are made as a judicial claim (da'wa) before a judge that the claimant cannot prove. The key distinction: for a claim of theft, if unproven, no ta'zir, because the intent is to recover property, not to accuse of theft. For a claim of zina, however, even if made as a judicial claim, the accuser is punished because zina cannot be proven without witnesses, and the mere claim attributes zina to the accused. The passage then outlines specific insulting terms, each with its ruling: 'O hypocrite,' 'O disbeliever,' 'O Jew' all lead to ta'zir when said abusively, but not if said as a claim. 'O Rafidi' is like 'O disbeliever' if it implies insulting the Companions. 'O fasiq' and 'O fajir' are distinct, and testimony using them interchangeably is invalid. For 'O Loti', Abu Hanifa says ta'zir, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say hadd for qadhf (slander of zina). The relied-upon position is ta'zir when said in anger. Other terms like 'dayyuth' (cuckold) and 'qurtaban' (pimp) also result in ta'zir. The term 'son of a prostitute' (ibn al-qahba) is complex: if said to a person's parent, it is ta'zir; if said to a wife, ta'zir, not hadd, because 'qahba' is not explicit for zina in the legal sense, but in custom may imply it. The passage reconciles opinions and emphasizes that ta'zir is the default for insults, while hadd for qadhf requires explicit accusation of zina.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment at the judge's discretion for offenses that have no fixed hadd or kaffarah.
قذف (qadhf): Slander, specifically false accusation of zina or of illegitimate birth, punishable by hadd (80 lashes) if proven or confessed.
شتم (sabb/شتم): Insult, abuse, or reviling; a general category of offensive speech that incurs ta'zir.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or suit brought before a judge; if the claim implies an accusation (like zina) and cannot be proven, the claimant is punished.
قحبة (qahba): A prostitute who openly commits adultery for payment; considered more obscene than zaniya (adulteress).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person calls his wife 'ya qahba', what is the ruling?
A) He is subject to hadd for qadhf.
B) He is ta'zir-ed.
C) No punishment.
D) He is executed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, insulting speech is punished by ta'zir unless it is an explicit accusation of zina; even judicial claims of theft do not incur ta'zir, but false claims of zina always do.

Timing of Return and the Hadd
Explains four scenarios of return timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of returning stolen property and apply the Hanafi ruling on whether amputation is applied in each scenario.

Core Idea
Returning stolen property before litigation prevents amputation; returning after does not.

In Hanafi criminal law, the moment a thief returns stolen goods critically determines whether the hadd of amputation is enforced. This lesson breaks down the four possible timings relative to litigation and clarifies when the return saves the hand.

Guided Path
1
Define litigation (khusumah)
Understand that litigation means the case is established before the judge through a formal claim and evidence (testimony or confession).
Without proper litigation, the theft is not legally established for hadd.
2
Identify the four scenarios
Return can occur: (1) after going to court but before a claim, (2) after a claim but before proof, (3) after proof but before judgment, (4) after judgment.
The first two are before litigation is complete; the last two after.
3
Apply the ruling
No amputation in the first two scenarios; amputation in the last two.
Once the case is proven (by testimony/confession), the hadd becomes wajib and return does not remove it.
4
Note the madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that return after proof still prevents hadd; the Hanafi school distinguishes by timing based on istihsan.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه أو ملكه بعد القضاء أو ادعى أنه ملكه أو نقصت قيمته مقصودها فتبقى تقديراً ؛ كذا في الهداية وهو شامل لما إذا رده بعد القضاء بالقطع وما إذا رده بعد ما شهد الشهود ولم يقض القاضي استحساناً لأن السرقة قد ظهرت عند القاضي بما هو حجة بناء على خصومة معتبرة ؛ كذا في التبيين. فالمراد بالخصومة الدعوى والشهادة أو الإقرار، فلو ادعى ولم يثبت ثم رده ينبغي أن لا قطع لعدم ظهورها عند القاضي فهي رباعية لأن الرد إما أن يكون بعد الترافع إلى القاضي قبل الدعوى أو بعدها قبل الثبوت أو بعدهما قبل القضاء أو بعد الثلاثة، فلا قطع في الأوليين ويقطع في الأخرين. وأطلق في الرد فشمل الرد حقيقة والرد حكماً كما إذا رده إلى أصوله وإن علا كوالده وجده ووالدته وجدته، سواء كانوا في عيال المالك أو لا، لأن لهؤلاء شبهة الملك فيثبت به شبهة الرد بخلاف ما إذا رده إلى عيال أصوله فإنه يقطع لأنه شبهة الشبهة وهي غير معتبرة. ومن الرد الحكمي إليه الرد إلى فرعه وكل ذي رحم محرم منه بشرط أن يكون في عياله وإلا فليس برد ومنه الرد إلى مكاتبه وعبده، ومنه الرد إلى مولاه لو كان مكاتباً لأن ماله له رقبة ومنه إذا سرق من العيال ورد إلى من يعولهم لأن يده عليهم فوق أيديهم في ماله. الثانية لو ملكه بعد القضاء بالقطع فلان الإمضاء من القضاء في هذا الباب لوقوع الاستغناء عنه بالاستيفاء إذ القضاء للإظهار والقطع حق الله تعالى وهو ظاهر عنده، وإذا كان كذلك يشترط قيام الخصومة عند الاستيفاء وصار كما إذا ملكها منه قبل القضاء أطلقه فشمل البيع والهبة لكن يشترط القبض فيها ليحصل الملك كما في الهداية الثالثة لو ادعى السارق أن المسروق ملكه بعد ما ثبتت السرقة عليه بالبينة أو بالإقرار فلا قطع سواء أقام بينة أو لم يقم، لأن الشبهة دارئة للحد فتتحقق بمجرد الدعوى بدليل صحة الرجوع بعد الإقرار الرابعة إذا سرق شيئاً قيمته نصاب ثم نقصت قيمته بعد القضاء لم يقطع لأن كمال النصاب لما كان شرطاً يشترط قيامه عند الإمضاء لما ذكرنا . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تغير السعر في بلد أو بلدين حتى إذا سرق ما قيمته نصاب في بلد وأخذ في بلد آخر القيمة فيه أنقص لم يقطع كما في شرح الطحاوي. وقيد بنقصان القيمة لأن العين لو نقصت فإنه يقطع لأنه مضمون عليه فكمل النصاب عيناً وديناً كما إذا استهلكه كله، أما نقصان السعر فغير مضمون فافترقا .
The book of theft: And his returning it before litigation to its owner, or his owning it after judgment, or his claiming that it is his property, or its value decreased — its intended meaning remains approximate; as in al-Hidayah. And it includes when he returns it after the judgment of amputation and when he returns it after the witnesses testified but the judge did not yet rule, by istihsan, because the theft has become apparent to the judge by a proof based on valid litigation; as in al-Tabyin. So what is meant by litigation is the claim and testimony or confession. So if he claimed but did not prove, then returned it, it is proper that there is no amputation due to lack of its appearance to the judge. So it is fourfold: because the return is either after litigation before the claim, or after it before proof, or after both before judgment, or after the three. So there is no amputation in the first two, and amputation in the latter two. And he made the return absolute, so it includes actual return and constructive return, as when he returns it to his ancestors, even if high, like his father, grandfather, mother, grandmother, whether they are dependents of the owner or not, because for these there is a semblance of ownership, so a semblance of return is established. Unlike if he returns it to the dependents of his ancestors, for he is amputated because it is a semblance of a semblance, which is not considered. And from constructive return is return to his descendant and every blood relative prohibited by marriage, on condition that he is in his dependency, otherwise it is not a return. And from it is return to his mukatab and his slave. And from it is return to his master if he was a mukatab, because his property is for the master. And from it is if he steals from dependents and returns to the one who supports them, because his authority over them is above their authority in his property. The second: if he owns it after the amputation judgment, then the execution of the judgment in this matter is for the occurrence of independence from it by fulfillment, since judgment is for manifestation and amputation is a right of Allah, and it is apparent with him. And when that is the case, the presence of litigation at the time of execution is required, and it becomes as if he owned it from him before judgment. He made it absolute, so it includes sale and gift, but delivery is required in them to acquire ownership, as in al-Hidayah. The third: if the thief claims that the stolen property is his after the theft is proven against him by evidence or by confession, then no amputation, whether he brings evidence or not, because the semblance wards off the hadd, so it is realized merely by the claim, as evidenced by the validity of retraction after confession. The fourth: if he steals something whose value is a nisab, then its value decreases after the judgment, he is not amputated because completeness of nisab is a condition, so its presence at the time of execution is required, as we mentioned. He made it absolute, so it includes if the price changes in one country or two, so that if he steals something worth a nisab in a country and is seized in another country where the value is less, he is not amputated, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And he restricted it to decrease in value because if the physical item decreased, he is amputated because it is guaranteed against him, so the nisab is complete physically and as debt, as if he consumed it all. As for decrease in price, it is not guaranteed, so they differ.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It details rulings on returning stolen property and its effect on the hadd of amputation. The author explains that the return of the stolen item can prevent amputation only if done before litigation (i.e., before the case is established before the judge). If returned after witnesses have testified or after judgment, the hadd remains. The commentary breaks down four scenarios based on when the return occurs, and also covers constructive return (such as to ancestors or dependents) which carries a semblance of ownership. The discussion also addresses claims of ownership after proof: mere claim creates doubt (shubha) that wards off the hadd. Finally, if the value of the stolen item drops below nisab after the judgment (e.g., due to market fluctuation), no amputation is applied because the nisab condition must be met at the time of execution. The distinction is made between physical decrease (which is guaranteed, so nisab considered complete) and price decrease (not guaranteed).

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): Returning the stolen property to the owner or its constructive return to those with a semblance of ownership.
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation: the claim and testimony or confession that establish the case before the judge.
شبهة (shubhah): Semblance or doubt that prevents application of hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief returns the stolen property after the judge has passed the amputation verdict, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Amputation is waived because the property was returned
B) Amputation is still applied because the return occurred after judgment
C) Amputation is commuted to a fine
D) It depends on whether the owner accepts the return

Key Takeaway
The moment of return relative to litigation is decisive: return before litigation prevents amputation; return after proof or judgment does not.

Constructive Return and Semblance of Ownership
Explains constructive return and when it prevents amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify what constitutes constructive return (radd hukmi) in Hanafi law and explain why returning to certain relatives creates a semblance that prevents amputation.

Core Idea
Returning stolen property to ancestors or dependents is considered valid constructive return that prevents amputation.

The Hanafi school extends the concept of 'return' beyond physical delivery to include constructive return — giving the stolen item to those who have a semblance of ownership, such as the thief's ancestors or dependents. This lesson explores the rationale and limits of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand constructive return (radd hukmi)
Constructive return means the thief places the stolen property into the hands of someone who stands in a legal relationship to the owner, such that it is as if the owner received it.
The key is 'shubhat al-milk' (semblance of ownership).
2
Identify who qualifies
Ancestors (parents, grandparents) and descendants, as well as blood relatives within prohibited degrees (mahram), provided they are dependents of the owner.
Return to non-dependent relatives does not count because the semblance is weaker.
3
Apply the rule of 'shubhat al-shubhah'
Returning to the dependents of ancestors (e.g., the servant of one's grandfather) is not valid because it is a semblance of a semblance, which is too weak to prevent hadd.
The threshold for shubha is single-level connection.
4
Recognize other forms
Return to one's own slave or mukatab counts as constructive return because the slave's property is considered the master's.
Similarly, returning to the guardian of one's dependents (if one supports them) is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه أو ملكه بعد القضاء أو ادعى أنه ملكه أو نقصت قيمته مقصودها فتبقى تقديراً ؛ كذا في الهداية وهو شامل لما إذا رده بعد القضاء بالقطع وما إذا رده بعد ما شهد الشهود ولم يقض القاضي استحساناً لأن السرقة قد ظهرت عند القاضي بما هو حجة بناء على خصومة معتبرة ؛ كذا في التبيين. فالمراد بالخصومة الدعوى والشهادة أو الإقرار، فلو ادعى ولم يثبت ثم رده ينبغي أن لا قطع لعدم ظهورها عند القاضي فهي رباعية لأن الرد إما أن يكون بعد الترافع إلى القاضي قبل الدعوى أو بعدها قبل الثبوت أو بعدهما قبل القضاء أو بعد الثلاثة، فلا قطع في الأوليين ويقطع في الأخرين. وأطلق في الرد فشمل الرد حقيقة والرد حكماً كما إذا رده إلى أصوله وإن علا كوالده وجده ووالدته وجدته، سواء كانوا في عيال المالك أو لا، لأن لهؤلاء شبهة الملك فيثبت به شبهة الرد بخلاف ما إذا رده إلى عيال أصوله فإنه يقطع لأنه شبهة الشبهة وهي غير معتبرة. ومن الرد الحكمي إليه الرد إلى فرعه وكل ذي رحم محرم منه بشرط أن يكون في عياله وإلا فليس برد ومنه الرد إلى مكاتبه وعبده، ومنه الرد إلى مولاه لو كان مكاتباً لأن ماله له رقبة ومنه إذا سرق من العيال ورد إلى من يعولهم لأن يده عليهم فوق أيديهم في ماله. الثانية لو ملكه بعد القضاء بالقطع فلان الإمضاء من القضاء في هذا الباب لوقوع الاستغناء عنه بالاستيفاء إذ القضاء للإظهار والقطع حق الله تعالى وهو ظاهر عنده، وإذا كان كذلك يشترط قيام الخصومة عند الاستيفاء وصار كما إذا ملكها منه قبل القضاء أطلقه فشمل البيع والهبة لكن يشترط القبض فيها ليحصل الملك كما في الهداية الثالثة لو ادعى السارق أن المسروق ملكه بعد ما ثبتت السرقة عليه بالبينة أو بالإقرار فلا قطع سواء أقام بينة أو لم يقم، لأن الشبهة دارئة للحد فتتحقق بمجرد الدعوى بدليل صحة الرجوع بعد الإقرار الرابعة إذا سرق شيئاً قيمته نصاب ثم نقصت قيمته بعد القضاء لم يقطع لأن كمال النصاب لما كان شرطاً يشترط قيامه عند الإمضاء لما ذكرنا . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تغير السعر في بلد أو بلدين حتى إذا سرق ما قيمته نصاب في بلد وأخذ في بلد آخر القيمة فيه أنقص لم يقطع كما في شرح الطحاوي. وقيد بنقصان القيمة لأن العين لو نقصت فإنه يقطع لأنه مضمون عليه فكمل النصاب عيناً وديناً كما إذا استهلكه كله، أما نقصان السعر فغير مضمون فافترقا .

الرد الحكمي (al-radd al-hukmi): Constructive return; returning stolen property to someone with a legal connection to the owner.
شبهة الملك (shubhat al-milk): Semblance of ownership; a reasonable appearance of ownership that creates doubt (shubha).
عيال (iyal): Dependents; those whom one supports financially.

Return After Judgment, Claim of Ownership, and Decrease in Value
Rules on ownership after judgment, claim of ownership, and decrease in value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ownership after judgment does not prevent amputation, why a mere claim of ownership creates shubha that prevents hadd, and how decrease in value below nisab after judgment affects the penalty.

Core Idea
After judgment, neither owning the property nor a claim of ownership prevents amputation, but a decrease in value below nisab does.

Beyond the initial timing of return, Hanafi law addresses three additional situations: when the thief acquires ownership of the stolen item after judgment, when he claims it as his own after proof, and when its value drops below the nisab threshold. Each has distinct rulings rooted in the concepts of shubha and the maintenance of the nisab condition.

Guided Path
1
Ownership after judgment
If the thief comes to own the stolen property after the amputation judgment (e.g., by purchase or gift), the hadd is still applied because the judgment is for execution, and the hadd is a right of Allah that does not abate by subsequent ownership. However, delivery of the item is required for the transfer of ownership to be effective.
Ownership before judgment could create shubha; after judgment, it does not.
2
Claim of ownership after proof
If the thief claims after the theft is proven (by evidence or confession) that the item is his own, the hadd is not applied, whether he brings evidence or not. The mere claim creates a shubha (doubt) that wards off the hadd, similar to the validity of retracting a confession.
This is based on the principle that shubha prevents hadd.
3
Decrease in value after judgment
If the value of the stolen item drops below nisab after the judgment (e.g., due to market price change), no amputation is applied. The nisab condition must be present at the time of execution, not just at the time of theft.
If the physical item itself is damaged (not price fluctuation), the nisab is considered complete because the thief is liable for the value.
4
Distinguish physical decrease vs price decrease
If the stolen item itself becomes damaged (e.g., broken), the nisab is deemed complete because the thief guarantees the item. But a drop in market price is not guaranteed, so it affects the nisab condition.
This is a subtle but important distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ورده قبل الخصومة إلى مالكه أو ملكه بعد القضاء أو ادعى أنه ملكه أو نقصت قيمته مقصودها فتبقى تقديراً ؛ كذا في الهداية وهو شامل لما إذا رده بعد القضاء بالقطع وما إذا رده بعد ما شهد الشهود ولم يقض القاضي استحساناً لأن السرقة قد ظهرت عند القاضي بما هو حجة بناء على خصومة معتبرة ؛ كذا في التبيين. فالمراد بالخصومة الدعوى والشهادة أو الإقرار، فلو ادعى ولم يثبت ثم رده ينبغي أن لا قطع لعدم ظهورها عند القاضي فهي رباعية لأن الرد إما أن يكون بعد الترافع إلى القاضي قبل الدعوى أو بعدها قبل الثبوت أو بعدهما قبل القضاء أو بعد الثلاثة، فلا قطع في الأوليين ويقطع في الأخرين. وأطلق في الرد فشمل الرد حقيقة والرد حكماً كما إذا رده إلى أصوله وإن علا كوالده وجده ووالدته وجدته، سواء كانوا في عيال المالك أو لا، لأن لهؤلاء شبهة الملك فيثبت به شبهة الرد بخلاف ما إذا رده إلى عيال أصوله فإنه يقطع لأنه شبهة الشبهة وهي غير معتبرة. ومن الرد الحكمي إليه الرد إلى فرعه وكل ذي رحم محرم منه بشرط أن يكون في عياله وإلا فليس برد ومنه الرد إلى مكاتبه وعبده، ومنه الرد إلى مولاه لو كان مكاتباً لأن ماله له رقبة ومنه إذا سرق من العيال ورد إلى من يعولهم لأن يده عليهم فوق أيديهم في ماله. الثانية لو ملكه بعد القضاء بالقطع فلان الإمضاء من القضاء في هذا الباب لوقوع الاستغناء عنه بالاستيفاء إذ القضاء للإظهار والقطع حق الله تعالى وهو ظاهر عنده، وإذا كان كذلك يشترط قيام الخصومة عند الاستيفاء وصار كما إذا ملكها منه قبل القضاء أطلقه فشمل البيع والهبة لكن يشترط القبض فيها ليحصل الملك كما في الهداية الثالثة لو ادعى السارق أن المسروق ملكه بعد ما ثبتت السرقة عليه بالبينة أو بالإقرار فلا قطع سواء أقام بينة أو لم يقم، لأن الشبهة دارئة للحد فتتحقق بمجرد الدعوى بدليل صحة الرجوع بعد الإقرار الرابعة إذا سرق شيئاً قيمته نصاب ثم نقصت قيمته بعد القضاء لم يقطع لأن كمال النصاب لما كان شرطاً يشترط قيامه عند الإمضاء لما ذكرنا . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تغير السعر في بلد أو بلدين حتى إذا سرق ما قيمته نصاب في بلد وأخذ في بلد آخر القيمة فيه أنقص لم يقطع كما في شرح الطحاوي. وقيد بنقصان القيمة لأن العين لو نقصت فإنه يقطع لأنه مضمون عليه فكمل النصاب عيناً وديناً كما إذا استهلكه كله، أما نقصان السعر فغير مضمون فافترقا .
The book of theft: And his returning it before litigation to its owner, or his owning it after judgment, or his claiming that it is his property, or its value decreased — its intended meaning remains approximate; as in al-Hidayah. And it includes when he returns it after the judgment of amputation and when he returns it after the witnesses testified but the judge did not yet rule, by istihsan, because the theft has become apparent to the judge by a proof based on valid litigation; as in al-Tabyin. So what is meant by litigation is the claim and testimony or confession. So if he claimed but did not prove, then returned it, it is proper that there is no amputation due to lack of its appearance to the judge. So it is fourfold: because the return is either after litigation before the claim, or after it before proof, or after both before judgment, or after the three. So there is no amputation in the first two, and amputation in the latter two. And he made the return absolute, so it includes actual return and constructive return, as when he returns it to his ancestors, even if high, like his father, grandfather, mother, grandmother, whether they are dependents of the owner or not, because for these there is a semblance of ownership, so a semblance of return is established. Unlike if he returns it to the dependents of his ancestors, for he is amputated because it is a semblance of a semblance, which is not considered. And from constructive return is return to his descendant and every blood relative prohibited by marriage, on condition that he is in his dependency, otherwise it is not a return. And from it is return to his mukatab and his slave. And from it is return to his master if he was a mukatab, because his property is for the master. And from it is if he steals from dependents and returns to the one who supports them, because his authority over them is above their authority in his property. The second: if he owns it after the amputation judgment, then the execution of the judgment in this matter is for the occurrence of independence from it by fulfillment, since judgment is for manifestation and amputation is a right of Allah, and it is apparent with him. And when that is the case, the presence of litigation at the time of execution is required, and it becomes as if he owned it from him before judgment. He made it absolute, so it includes sale and gift, but delivery is required in them to acquire ownership, as in al-Hidayah. The third: if the thief claims that the stolen property is his after the theft is proven against him by evidence or by confession, then no amputation, whether he brings evidence or not, because the semblance wards off the hadd, so it is realized merely by the claim, as evidenced by the validity of retraction after confession. The fourth: if he steals something whose value is a nisab, then its value decreases after the judgment, he is not amputated because completeness of nisab is a condition, so its presence at the time of execution is required, as we mentioned. He made it absolute, so it includes if the price changes in one country or two, so that if he steals something worth a nisab in a country and is seized in another country where the value is less, he is not amputated, as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And he restricted it to decrease in value because if the physical item decreased, he is amputated because it is guaranteed against him, so the nisab is complete physically and as debt, as if he consumed it all. As for decrease in price, it is not guaranteed, so they differ.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It details rulings on returning stolen property and its effect on the hadd of amputation. The author explains that the return of the stolen item can prevent amputation only if done before litigation (i.e., before the case is established before the judge). If returned after witnesses have testified or after judgment, the hadd remains. The commentary breaks down four scenarios based on when the return occurs, and also covers constructive return (such as to ancestors or dependents) which carries a semblance of ownership. The discussion also addresses claims of ownership after proof: mere claim creates doubt (shubha) that wards off the hadd. Finally, if the value of the stolen item drops below nisab after the judgment (e.g., due to market fluctuation), no amputation is applied because the nisab condition must be met at the time of execution. The distinction is made between physical decrease (which is guaranteed, so nisab considered complete) and price decrease (not guaranteed).

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): Returning the stolen property to the owner or its constructive return.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum value threshold for theft (approximately 10 dirhams).
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Doubt or semblance that wards off the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Amputation is not applied because the nisab must be present at execution
C) Amputation is applied only if the thief caused the decrease
D) Amputation is commuted to a fine

Key Takeaway
The nisab condition at execution, the effect of shubha, and the irrelevance of subsequent ownership are key to these rulings.

Joint Confession and Retraction: Shubha Saves Both
Ruling on joint confession with retraction by claiming ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one of two joint confessors to theft retracts by claiming ownership, and explain why the shubha extends to both partners.

Core Idea
When partners confess jointly, one partner's retraction creates shubha for all — the hadd is averted for both.

In Hanafi criminal law, the hadd punishment for theft (amputation) is averted by even a weak semblance (shubha). This lesson examines a specific scenario: two people confess together to stealing a specific item, then one of them claims the item is his property. The commentator explains that this retraction creates shubha not only for the retractor but also for the other confessor, because the original confession established a partnership in the theft. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly applying the rules of confession and retraction in hudud cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two persons confess jointly to theft of a specific item. Then one of them says 'the item is my property' – a retraction of the confession.
The retraction can occur before or after judgment.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Both confessors are not amputated (the hadd is averted for both).
This applies whether the retraction is before or after judgment, as the text says ' أطلقه '.
3
Analyze the legal basis (dalil)
The joint confession establishes a partnership in the theft. The retraction of one injects doubt (shubha) into the entire case. Since the hadd is averted by doubt, both are saved.
The shubha is created by the claim of ownership, which is a valid legal right.
4
Distinguish from the case of a single confessor with a denier
If only one person confesses and names an accomplice who denies, then only the confessor is amputated. There is no joint confession, so no partnership and no shubha for the denier.
The text gives the analogy: 'I and so-and-so killed' – similar to theft confession.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical effect)
In Hanafi fiqh, both go free. In other views (e.g., Shafi'i), perhaps only the retractor is saved, while the other remains liable.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that shubha extends to all parties in a joint confession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو أقرا بسرقة ثم قال أحدهما هو مالي لم يقطعا) أي السارقان المقران لأن الرجوع عامل في حق الراجع ومورث للشبهة في حق الآخر لأن السرقة قد ثبتت بإقرارهما على الشركة أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قبل القضاء أو بعده وقيد بإقرارهما لأنه لو أقر أنه سرق هو وفلان كذا فأنكر فلان فإنه يقطع المقر لعدم الشركة بتكذيبه بقوله قتلت أنا وفلان الفتح.
His saying: 'And if two confess to theft, then one of them says it is my property, they are not amputated' — i.e., the two confessing thieves, because the retraction is effective in the right of the retractor and creates a semblance for the other, because the theft has been proven by their joint confession. He made it absolute, so it includes whether it is before or after judgment. And he restricted it to their confession, because if he confessed that he and so-and-so stole, and so-and-so denied, then the confessor is amputated due to lack of partnership due to the denial, as in his saying 'I and so-and-so killed' — al-Fath.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding two people who confess to theft, then one of them retracts by claiming the stolen item is his property. The commentator explains that both are not amputated because the retraction of one creates a semblance (shubha) for the other as well. This applies whether the retraction is before or after judgment. However, if only one person confesses and names an accomplice who denies, then the confessor alone is amputated because there is no joint confession to establish the theft against the other. The example given is akin to a confession of murder: if one says 'I and so-and-so killed', and the other denies, only the confessor is liable.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Retraction from a confession or claim, here by claiming ownership of the stolen item.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Semblance or doubt that prevents application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Both are saved from amputation
C) Both are amputated because confession was made
D) The other is amputated but the claimant is spared

Key Takeaway
In joint confession, retraction by one creates shubha for all partners, averting the hadd.

Absent Thief: Amputation of the Present Thief Only
Ruling when one thief absents after theft and testimony covers both

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one thief is absent and witnesses testify against both, and explain why the absent thief's absence does not create a shubha that saves the present thief.

Core Idea
Absence of one thief does not prevent amputation of the present thief; only actual shubha wards off hadd, not mere possibility.

This passage deals with a scenario where two thieves commit a theft, but only one is present in court. The witnesses testify against both. Are both punished? The Hanafi ruling is clear: only the present thief faces amputation. The reason lies in the concept of absence preventing the establishment of theft against the absent one, and the principle that only existing shubha (semblance) blocks the hadd, not a hypothetical future semblance. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the key term 'shubhat al-shubhah'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Note that two persons steal, one is absent, witnesses testify against both. The ruling: cut off the hand of the present thief only.
2
Understand the reasoning: absence prevents proof
Absence of the thief prevents the prosecution from establishing the theft against him in court. He is treated as legally non-existent for this case.
Theft must be proven against the specific individual; absence blocks the legal process.
3
Analyze the shubha argument
Could the absent thief's potential future claim of ownership create a shubha for the present thief? The text says no, because that would be a 'shubhat al-shubhah' (semblance of a semblance) — too remote to consider.
Only an existing, actual shubha can ward off the hadd, not a hypothetical possibility.
4
Apply the principle
The present thief cannot benefit from the absent thief's absence. The hadd is applied immediately to him.
The ruling is definitive: the present thief is amputated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو سرقا وغاب أحدهما وشهدا على سرقتهما قطع الآخر) أي الحاضر لأن الغيبة تمنع ثبوت السرقة على الغائب فيبقى معدوماً والعدم لا يورث الشبهة، ولا معتبر بتوهم حدوث الشبهة لأنه شبهة الشبهة وبيانه أن الغائب لو حضر وادعى كان شبهة للحاضر واحتمال دعوى الغائب شبهة الشبهة فلا تعتبر.
His saying: 'And if two steal and one of them is absent, and witnesses testify against both, the other (the present one) is amputated' — i.e., the present one, because absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent one, so he remains non-existent, and non-existence does not produce a semblance. And consideration is not given to the possibility of a semblance arising, because it is a semblance of a semblance. Its explanation: if the absent one were present and claimed, that would be a semblance for the present one; but the mere possibility of the absent one's claim is a semblance of a semblance, so it is not considered.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where two people commit theft, but only one is present in court while the other is absent. Witnesses testify against both. The ruling: only the present thief is amputated. The reason is that the absent thief cannot have the theft proven against him in his absence, so he is legally 'non-existent' for this case. Since the theft is not established against him, he cannot create a semblance (shubha) for the present thief. The commentary emphasizes that mere imagination that the absent thief might later claim ownership (which would create shubha) is not considered, because it is a 'semblance of a semblance,' too remote to ward off the hadd.

Key Terms
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat al-shubhah): A remote possibility that a semblance (shubha) might arise; not considered as actual shubha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, what is the consequence for the present thief when witnesses testify against both him and an absent accomplice?
A) He is not punished until the absent thief is found
B) He is amputated because his theft is proven and the absent thief's absence does not create a valid shubha
C) He is punished only if the absent thief confesses
D) He is punished but the amputation is delayed until the absent thief is brought to court

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the presence of an absent accomplice does not shield the present thief from hadd; only an actual, existing shubha can prevent the punishment.

Slave Confessing to Theft: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on a slave's confession to theft and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's confession to theft and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interdicted slave.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft is valid for hadd and property return; the interdicted slave's amputation is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This lesson examines the legal status of a slave's confession to theft. While a slave is considered property in financial matters, he is a human being for hudud, so his confession is accepted. The passage also highlights a difference between Imam Muhammad and the other Hanafi imams regarding the interdicted slave, with the majority view prevailing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine the act (slave confesses to theft) and the ruling (amputation wajib, return of stolen item if extant).
The ruling applies to any adult slave who confesses voluntarily.
2
Understand the legal basis
The confession is valid because the slave is a human being for hadd; property follows as subsidiary.
No suspicion of falsehood since confession harms the slave.
3
Distinguish slave types
The ruling applies to both authorized (ma'dhun) and interdicted (mahjur) slaves, but Imam Muhammad differs on the interdicted.
Authorized slaves can be liable for property, interdicted cannot.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Muhammad al-Shaybani says the interdicted slave is not amputated; Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa disagree.
The difference is only on amputation, not on property return.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that the slave is amputated in all cases.
The text states the ruling in absolute terms, indicating generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو أقر عبد بسرقة قطع وترد السرقة إلى المسروق منه) لأن إقرار العبد على نفسه بالحدود والقصاص صحيح من حيث إنه آدمي ثم يتعدى إلى المالية فيصح من حيث إنه مال، ولأنه لا تهمة في هذا الإقرار لما يشتمل عليه من الإضرار ومثله مقبول على الغير فيقطع العبد . وإذا صح الإقرار بالقطع صح بالمال بناء عليه لأن الإقرار يلاقي حالة البقاء والمال في حالة البقاء تابع فقط حتى تسقط عصمة المال باعتباره ويستوفي القطع بعد استهلاكه أطلق العبد فشمل المأذون والمحجور عليه وخالف محمد في المحجور فقال : لا يقطع وخالفه أبو يوسف، واتفقا على أن المال للمولى. وأطلق في القطع فشمل ما إذا صدقه المولى وكذبه والخلاف فيه فقط. وأطلق في السرقة فشمل القائمة والمستهلكة. وأشار بالرد المقيد لبقائها إلى أنها لو كانت مستهلكة فلا ضمان ويقطع وأشار بالقطع إلى أن العبد كبير إذ لا قطع إلا على مكلف فإذا أقر عبد صغير بسرقة فلا قطع غير أنه إذا كان مأذوناً يرد المال إلى المسروق منه إن كان قائماً، وإن كان هالكاً يضمن، وإن كان محجوراً فإن صدقه المولى يرد المال إلى المسروق منه إن كان قائماً لا ضمان عليه إن كان هالكاً ولا بعد العتق ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقيد بالإقرار ليفيد أن السرقة لو ثبتت عليه بالبينة فإنه يقطع بالأولى ويرد المال إلى المسروق منه كما في الذخيرة، لكن يشترط حضرة المولى عند إقامة البينة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف ليست بشرط وأما حضرته عند الإقرار بالحدود فليست بشرط اتفاقاً؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي.
His saying: 'And if a slave confesses to theft, he is amputated and the stolen item is returned to the victim' — because the slave's confession against himself regarding hudud and qisas is valid inasmuch as he is a human being; then it extends to financial matters, so it is valid inasmuch as he is property. And because there is no suspicion in this confession, as it involves harm to himself, and a similar confession is accepted against others, so the slave is amputated. And if the confession for amputation is valid, it is also valid for the property based on that, because the confession meets the state of remaining, and the property is only subsidiary in that state. So the financial protection is removed by the confession, and the amputation is carried out even after the stolen item is consumed. He made the slave absolute, so it includes the authorized slave and the interdicted one. Muhammad differed regarding the interdicted: he said he is not amputated, and Abu Yusuf disagreed with him. They agreed that the property goes to the master. And he made the amputation absolute, so it includes whether the master believes him or denies him, the difference is only in that. And he made the theft absolute, so it includes the still-existing and the consumed. And he indicated by the restricted return (only if still extant) that if it is consumed, there is no liability (for the slave) and he is amputated. And he indicated by the amputation that the slave must be an adult, because amputation is only for the legally responsible. So if a minor slave confesses to theft, there is no amputation, except that if he is authorized, the property is returned to the victim if extant, and if consumed, he is liable. If he is interdicted and the master believes him, the property is returned if extant; there is no liability if consumed, not even after emancipation, as in Fath al-Qadir. And he restricted it to confession to indicate that if the theft is proven against him by evidence, he is amputated a fortiori and the property is returned to the victim, as in al-Dhakhira. However, the presence of the master is required for establishing evidence according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf said it is not a condition. As for his presence at the confession regarding hudud, it is not a condition by agreement.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling of a slave confessing to theft. The Hanafi position: a slave's confession is accepted for hudud (amputation) because he is a human being, and it extends to financial liability because his confession also affects property rights. The slave is amputated and the stolen goods are returned to the victim if still extant. If consumed, there is no financial liability on the slave (since he is incapable of owning property), but the amputation stands. The commentary notes an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interdicted (mahjur) slave: Muhammad al-Shaybani holds he is not amputated, while Abu Yusuf disagrees; the common view is that the slave is amputated. Additionally, if the theft is proven by evidence (rather than confession), the master's presence is required at the time of evidence according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf. The passage also clarifies that a minor slave's confession does not lead to amputation, but financial return may apply if authorized.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
المحجور عليه (al-mahjur 'alayhi): A slave who is interdicted, i.e., prohibited from dealing in property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No amputation, but he must pay compensation
B) Amputation only if the item is still extant
C) Amputation and no liability for consumption
D) Neither amputation nor compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave's confession to theft is valid for hadd (amputation) and property return, with the majority view applying even to interdicted slaves.

Definition and Types of Siyasa (Policy) in Islamic Law
Lexical definition and classification of siyasa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the lexical and technical definition of siyasa and distinguish between just and unjust siyasa according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Just siyasa is part of Sharia; unjust siyasa is forbidden.

In this passage, the commentator notes that earlier Hanafi scholars did not explicitly define siyasa (policy). He then provides a lexical origin and a technical definition, followed by a crucial distinction: siyasa can be just (aligned with Sharia) or unjust (forbidden). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how Islamic law accommodates public policy and governance.

Guided Path
1
Trace the lexical origin
Identify the Arabic root (s-w-s) and its meanings: to manage, govern, or undertake an affair. The term 'siyasa' derives from this root, implying management and leadership.
The passage also mentions 'sus' meaning nature or character, indicating that siyasa is rooted in the ruler's inherent qualities.
2
Extract the technical definition
From al-Maqrizi's definition: siyasa is 'the law established for nurturing manners and interests and regulating wealth.' This shows that siyasa is a set of regulations aimed at public welfare.
The term 'qanun' (law) is used here, but it must be understood within an Islamic framework.
3
Recognize the two types
Just siyasa extracts rights from oppressors and is part of Sharia. Unjust siyasa is condemned by Sharia. This binary classification is central to the Hanafi view.
The passage emphasizes that just siyasa is not separate from Sharia; it is integrated.
4
Understand the author's supplementary definition
The author quotes himself: siyasa is the ruler's action based on perceived benefit, even without specific textual evidence, as long as it aligns with Sharia principles.
This definition allows flexibility for the ruler to address new situations within the bounds of Sharia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم أر في كلام مشايخنا تعريف السياسة . قال المقريزي في الخطط : يقال ساس الأمر سياسة بمعنى قام به وهو سائس من قولهم ساسه وسوسه القوم جعلوه يسوسهم والسوس الطبع ،والخلق، يقال الفصاحة من سوسه والكرم من سوسه أي من طبعه. فهذا أصل وضع السياسة في اللغة، ثم رسمت بأنها القانون الموضوع لرعاية الآداب والمصالح وانتظام الأموال والسياسة نوعان : سياسة عادلة تخرج الحق من الظالم الفاجر فهي من الشريعة علمها من علمها وجهلها من جهلها، وقد صنف الناس في السياسة الشرعية كتباً متعددة، والنوع الآخر سياسة ظالمة فالشريعة تحرمها إلى آخر ما ذكره من النصف الثاني عند ذكر جيوش الدولة التركية والله تعالى أعلم . قوله : (ولم أر في كلام مشايخنا تعريف السياسة ذكر المؤلف في باب حد الزنا قبيل قول المتن والمريض يرجم ولا يجلد ما نصه : وظاهر كلامهم هنا أن السياسة هي فعل شيء من الحاكم لمصلحة يراها وإن لم يرد بذلك الفعل دليل جزئي اهـ .
I have not seen in the words of our scholars a definition of siyasa. Al-Maqrizi said in al-Khitat: It is said: sasa al-amr siyasatan meaning he undertook it, and he is a sa'is from their saying: he ruled him and they made him their ruler, and al-sus is nature and character. It is said: eloquence is from his nature, generosity is from his nature, i.e., from his nature. This is the origin of the word siyasa in language. Then it was defined as the law established for nurturing manners and interests and regulating wealth. Siyasa is of two types: just siyasa that extracts the right from the unjust wicked person, so it is from the Sharia; those who know it know it and those who ignore it ignore it. People have authored many books on al-siyasa al-shar'iyya. The other type is unjust siyasa, and the Sharia forbids it. Up to the end of what he mentioned from the second half when mentioning the armies of the Turkish state. And Allah knows best. The author said: (I have not seen in the words of our scholars a definition of siyasa. The author mentioned in the chapter of the prescribed punishment for zina, before the statement of the text: 'The sick person is stoned and not flogged', his words: And the apparent meaning of their words here is that siyasa is the doing of something by the ruler for an interest he sees, even if no particular evidence indicates that action.) End.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling lesson. The commentator notes that the earlier Hanafi scholars did not explicitly define siyasa (policy). He then cites al-Maqrizi's lexical and technical definition. Lexically, siyasa comes from sasa, meaning to manage or govern. Technically, it is a set of regulations for nurturing good manners, public interests, and property management. Siyasa is divided into two: just siyasa (siyasa 'adila), which extracts rights from oppressors and is in harmony with Sharia, and unjust siyasa, which is forbidden. The commentator emphasizes that just siyasa is not separate from Sharia; it is part of it. The author then quotes himself from the chapter on zina, where he defined siyasa as an action by the ruler based on his perception of benefit, even without specific textual evidence. This shows that siyasa operates within the framework of Sharia principles.

Key Terms
سياسة (siyasa): Policy: regulations set by a ruler for managing public affairs and interests, based on Sharia when just.
قانون (qanun): Law or regulation; here used in the definition of siyasa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The law established for nurturing manners, interests, and regulating wealth

Key Takeaway
Just siyasa is an integral part of Sharia, while unjust siyasa is forbidden; the ruler must ensure policies align with Islamic principles.
