Defining the Spoils (al-Salb): What is Included?
This passage defines salb with lexical and juristic commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of al-salb according to Hanafi custom and distinguish what is excluded.

Core Idea
Salb includes the mount, weapons, and personal belongings on the slain; items with servants or away from him are excluded.

After establishing the default ruling that salb is ghanimah, the text defines exactly what constitutes salb. This definition is crucial for determining what goes into the common pool. The commentary draws on lexicons (al-Mughrib, al-Qamus) and juristic custom to specify the scope. Things on the person, mount, and belt are included; items in his tent or with his servant are not. This lesson unpacks the definitional boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Definition Statement
The text says: 'And it is his mount, clothes, weapons, and what is with him.' This is the core definition of salb according to custom.
The definition is based on 'urf (custom), not explicit nass.
2
Break Down the Components
Mount includes saddle and equipment. 'What is with him' includes what is on his belt or on his animal. Clothes and weapons are straightforward.
The Hanafi custom is narrower than some other schools.
3
Identify Exclusions
Items with his servant, in his house, or tent are not salb. The key is personal carriage and the immediate context of combat.
This prevents over-expanding the definition.
4
Consider Lexical Variations
The text cites al-Mughrib, al-Layth, al-Azhari, and al-Qamus showing different shades of meaning, but the juristic definition is based on custom.
Lexicons help but fiqh custom prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر ما في الذخيرة عدم الحرمة قوله : (والسلب للكل إن لم ينفل) أي لا يختص به القاتل عندنا لأنه مأخوذ بقوة الجيش فيكون غنيمة فيقسم بينهم قسمة الغنائم كما نطق به النص وقال عليه السلام الحبيب بن أبي سلمة ليس لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك. وأما قوله عليه السلام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه» (۱) فيحتمل نصب الشرع ويحتمل التنفيل فنحمله على الثاني لما روينا قوله : (وهو) مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه) أي السلب ما ذكر للعرف. وفي المغرب : السلب المسلوب وعن الليث والأزهري : كل ما على الإنسان من اللباس فهو سلب وللفقهاء فيه كلام .اهـ. وفي القاموس السلب بالتحريك ما يسلب وجمعه أسلاب ودخل في مركبه ما كان عليه من سرج وآلة وما مع المقتول شامل لما كان في وسطه أو على دابته وما عدا ذلك مما هو مع غلامه أو في بيته أو في خيمته فليس بسلب. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان السلب عند المشرك عارية من صبي أو امرأة لأنه يستغنم مالهما كمال البالغ، وما إذا كان السلب ملكاً لمسلم دخل دارهم بأمان فغصبه المشرك المقتول لأنه ملكه بالاستيلاء فانقطع ملك المسلم عنه . ولو أخذ المشركون سلب المقتول ثم انهزموا فهو غنيمة ولا شيء للقاتل لأنهم ملكوه بالاستيلاء فبطل ملك القاتل ثم ملكه الغزاة وإن لم يدر أنهم أخذوه، فإن كان منزوعاً عنه فهو فيء لإثبات يدهم عليه بالنزع وإلا فهو للقاتل وإن جره المشركون أو حملوه على دابته وعليها سلاحه بخلاف ما إذا حملوا أسلحتهم وأمتعتهم عليها فإنه فيء. ولو وجد على دابة بعدما سار العسكر مرحلة أو مرحلتين ولا يدري أكان أحد في يد أو لا فهو للقاتل قياساً لا استحساناً ولو قال من قتل قتيلاً فله فرسه فقتل راجل راجلاً ومع غلامه فرسه قائم بجنبه بين الصفين يكون للقاتل فرسه إذا كان فرسه مع . غلامه بقرب منه لأن مقصود الإمام قتل من كان متمكناً من القتال فارساً وهذا كذلك، وإن لم يكن بجنبه في الصف فلا يكون له. ولو قتل مشركاً على برذون كان له لأنه يسمى فارساً، ولو كان على حمار أو بغل أو حمل لا يستحق السلب لأن راكب هذه الأشياء لا فارساً ولذا لا يسمى يستحق سهم الفارس؛ كذا في المحيط وبه علم أن ما ذكره الشارح عن المحيط بأنه قال الإمام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه سبق قلم، وإنما المذكور في المحيط فله فرسه والدليل عليه أنه ينبغي أن يباع السلاح منهم وقول المتن في الأيمان ومن حلف على معصية ينبغي أن يحنث) شائع في كلامهم قوله : (سبق (قلم قال الرملي : أي من بعض النساخ والذي في نسخنا من الزيلعي فله فرسه كما في المحيط (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب المفازي باب .٥٤ مسلم في كتاب الجهاد حديث ٤٢. أبو داود في كتاب الجهاد باب ١٣٦. الترمذي في كتاب السير باب ۱۳ . الموطأ في كتاب الجهاد حديث ١٨. أحمد في مسنده (١٢/٥، ٢٩٥) قال آخراً: لو كان راكباً على بغل ونحوه لا يكون له ولو كان التنفيل بلفظ السلب لاستحقه لأن المركب أعم منه ومن الفرس. قال في القاموس : المركب كمقعد واحد مراكب البر والبحر اهـ. وفي الهداية ثم حكم التنفيل قطع حق الباقين فأما الملك فإنما يثبت بعد الإحراز بدار الإسلام لما مر من قبل حتى لو قال الأمير من أصاب جارية فهي له فأصابها مسلم فاستبرأها لم يجز له وطؤها وكذا لا يبيعها هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد له أن يطاها ويبيعها لأن التنفيل يثبت به الملك عنده كما يثبت بالقسمة في دار الحرب والشراء من الحربي ووجوب الضمان بالإتلاف قد قيل على هذا الاختلاف اهـ. والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم .
That which is apparent in al-Dhakhira is that there is no prohibition. His statement: (And the spoils (salb) belong to all if he did not grant (tanfil)) meaning the killer is not exclusively entitled to it according to us because it is taken by the strength of the army, so it becomes booty (ghanimah) and is divided among them like the division of booty, as the text (Quran) declares. And the Prophet ﷺ said to Habib ibn Abi Salamah: 'You have nothing from the spoils of your slain except what your Imam willingly gives you.' As for his statement ﷺ: 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' – it could mean a prescribed share (nasb al-shar') or it could mean an extra grant (tanfil). We interpret it as the second based on what we narrated. His statement: (And it is his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him) i.e., al-salb is what was mentioned according to custom. In al-Mughrib: al-salb is the plundered goods. From al-Layth and al-Azhari: everything on a person of clothing is salb, but jurists have discussion about it. In al-Qamus: al-salb (with fath of the letter sin) is what is plundered, plural aslāb. Included in 'his mount' is what is on it of saddle and equipment, and 'what is with the slain' includes what was on his belt or on his animal. Anything else that is with his servant or in his house or tent is not salb. It is unrestricted, so it includes if the salb was with the polytheist as a loan from a child or woman, because their property is taken as booty like that of an adult. And if the salb was the property of a Muslim who entered their land with aman and the slain polytheist seized it, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, cutting off the Muslim's ownership. If the polytheists took the salb of the slain and then were defeated, it is booty and the killer gets nothing, because they owned it by seizure, so the killer's ownership is nullified, then the warriors own it, even if they don't know that they took it. If it was stripped from him, it is fay’ because they established their hand on it by stripping; otherwise it is for the killer. If the polytheists dragged or carried him on his animal with his weapons on it, it is different from if they carried their own weapons and belongings on it, which would be fay’. If something is found on an animal after the army has traveled one or two stages, and it is unknown whether anyone had it in hand or not, it belongs to the killer by qiyas, not istihsan. If someone says 'whoever kills a slain, he gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier while his servant has his horse standing near him between the ranks, the killer gets the horse if the horse was near his servant, because the Imam's intent is to kill one who is capable of fighting as a horseman, and this is so. But if it was not near him in the ranks, he does not get it. If he kills a polytheist on a baridhun (strong horse), he gets it because he is called a horseman. If he was on a donkey, mule, or ram, he does not deserve the salb because the rider of these things is not a horseman and thus does not deserve the horseman's share. In al-Muhit: thus it is known that what the commentator mentioned from al-Muhit that the Imam said 'whoever kills a slain gets his salb' is a slip of the pen; what is mentioned in al-Muhit is 'gets his horse'. Evidence is that the weapons should be sold from them. And the statement of the text in al-Ayman: 'whoever swears to commit a sin should break his oath' is common in their speech. His statement: (slip of the pen) – al-Ramli said: 'from some scribes, and in our copies from al-Zayla'i it is 'gets his horse' as in al-Muhit.' Narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Muwatta', Ahmad... He said further: if he was riding a mule or similar, he does not get it; but if the grant (tanfil) was worded as 'salb', he would deserve it because 'mount' (markab) is more general than horse. Al-Qamus says: al-markab (like masjad) refers to one of the mounts of land and sea. In al-Hidayah: then the ruling of tanfil is that it cuts off the right of others. As for ownership, it is only established after transport to dar al-Islam, as previously mentioned. So if the amir says 'whoever gets a slave girl, she is his', and a Muslim gets her and then performs istibra', he may not have intercourse with her, nor sell her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said he may have intercourse and sell her because, according to him, tanfil establishes ownership, just as ownership is established by division in dar al-harb and purchase from a harbi, and the obligation of guarantee for destruction is said to differ based on this difference. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the rules of al-salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy) and al-tanfil (extra grants by the Imam). The commentator begins by citing al-Dhakhira that there is no prohibition (apparently referring to a previous issue). The main ruling: the salb belongs to the entire army, not exclusively to the killer, because it is obtained through the collective strength of the army, thus it is ghanimah divided among all. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet told Habib ibn Abi Salamah that he only gets what the Imam gives him. The hadith 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' is interpreted as referring to tanfil (a discretionary grant), not as a fixed right, to reconcile with the first hadith. The commentator then defines salb: according to custom, it includes the slain's mount, clothes, weapons, and what he carries. He cites various lexicons: al-Mughrib, al-Layth, al-Azhari, and al-Qamus. He elaborates: the mount includes its saddle and equipment; 'what is with the slain' includes what is on his belt or animal. Anything with his servant, in his house, or tent is not salb. The rule applies whether the salb was borrowed from a child or woman, as their property is also booty. If the salb was originally a Muslim's property seized by the slain polytheist, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, so the killer does not get it. If the polytheists take the salb from the slain and then flee, the salb becomes general booty, not for the killer, because the polytheists had owned it by seizure. If the polytheists stripped the slain of his salb, it becomes fay' (property of the state). If they dragged him or his animal, the rider's possessions are for the killer, but their own weapons on the animal are fay'. If a salb is found on an animal after the army has moved one or two stages, it goes to the killer by qiyas (analogy) but not by istihsan (juristic preference). The text then addresses a specific case: if the Imam says 'whoever kills gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier whose horse is nearby with his servant, the killer gets the horse if it is close. If not, he doesn't. Killing a polytheist on a strong horse (bardhun) gives entitlement to the horse because he is a horseman; but on a donkey, mule, or ram, no. The commentator corrects a scribal error: 'salba' should be 'farasahu' (his horse) as in al-Muhit. The passage then discusses al-tanfil: it cuts off the right of others to the grant. Ownership under tanfil is only established after transport to dar al-Islam. There is an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say that if the Imam grants a slave girl, the recipient cannot have intercourse or sell her until after transport, because ownership is not yet complete. Muhammad says he can, as tanfil establishes immediate ownership in dar al-harb, similar to division or purchase.

Key Terms
السلب (al-salb): What is taken from a slain enemy including his mount, clothes, weapons, and what he carries.
المركب (al-markab): Mount, including any riding animal and its equipage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, which of the following is NOT considered part of al-salb?
A) The slain's sword in his belt.
B) The slain's horse with saddle.
C) The slain's gold coins in a chest in his tent.
D) The slain's armor he is wearing.

Key Takeaway
Salb is limited to what the slain personally carries on his body or mount; items with his servant, house, or tent are excluded.

Special Cases: When Ownership of Salb Changes
This passage details special cases modifying the basic rule of salb ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for special scenarios: when the salb was borrowed from a child/woman, when it was originally Muslim property seized by the enemy, and when the polytheists take it and are then defeated.

Core Idea
If polytheists take the salb and own it by seizure, the killer loses his right upon defeat; but if they merely strip it, it becomes fay'.

The text moves from the general definition to specific complex cases that test the boundary of ownership. What if the enemy was riding a borrowed animal from a child? What if the salb was originally a Muslim's goods taken by the enemy? What if the polytheists themselves take the salb from the slain and later are defeated? These scenarios illustrate the principles of seizure (istila') and how ownership transfers. This lesson analyzes these nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Case 1: Salb Borrowed from a Child or Woman
The text says the rule applies even if the salb was borrowed from a child or woman because the polytheist's property includes that which they took by force or use.
The polytheist's ownership by seizure is recognized.
2
Case 2: Salb Originally a Muslim's Property
If the slain had seized property from a Muslim who entered with aman, the property becomes the polytheist's by seizure, cutting off the Muslim's ownership. So it becomes ghanimah, not returned to the Muslim.
This is a harsh but consistent rule: seizure transfers ownership to the polytheist, then to the Muslim army as ghanimah.
3
Case 3: Polytheists Take Salb from the Slain and Then are Defeated
If polytheists take the salb from the slain, they own it by seizure. When the Muslim army defeats them and recaptures it, it is ghanimah for all, not exclusively for the original killer.
The killer's claim is nullified because the polytheists owned it.
4
Case 4: Distinction Between 'Stripping' and 'Carrying'
If the polytheists stripped the slalb from the slain (took it off his body), that act is similar to seizure, so it becomes fay'. But if they dragged him or his animal with the salb still on him, the salb remains for the killer (if he killed him) because they did not establish full ownership by stripping.
A fine distinction based on the level of possession.
5
Case 5: Found After Army Moves
If a salb is found on an animal after the army has traveled a stage or two, and it is unclear whether the polytheists had taken it, it belongs to the killer by qiyas, not istihsan.
This shows preference for analogy over juristic preference in ambiguous ownership.

Source Text

ظاهر ما في الذخيرة عدم الحرمة قوله : (والسلب للكل إن لم ينفل) أي لا يختص به القاتل عندنا لأنه مأخوذ بقوة الجيش فيكون غنيمة فيقسم بينهم قسمة الغنائم كما نطق به النص وقال عليه السلام الحبيب بن أبي سلمة That which is apparent in al-Dhakhira is that there is no prohibition. His statement: (And the spoils (salb) belong to all if he did not grant (tanfil)) meaning the killer is not exclusively entitled to it according to us because it is taken by the strength of the army, so it becomes booty (ghanimah) and is divided among them like the division of booty, as the text (Quran) declares. And the Prophet ﷺ said to Habib ibn Abi Salamah: 'You have nothing from the spoils of your slain except what your Imam willingly gives you.' As for his statement ﷺ: 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' – it could mean a prescribed share (nasb al-shar') or it could mean an extra grant (tanfil). We interpret it as the second based on what we narrated. His statement: (And it is his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him) i.e., al-salb is what was mentioned according to custom. In al-Mughrib: al-salb is the plundered goods. From al-Layth and al-Azhari: everything on a person of clothing is salb, but jurists have discussion about it. In al-Qamus: al-salb (with fath of the letter sin) is what is plundered, plural aslāb. Included in 'his mount' is what is on it of saddle and equipment, and 'what is with the slain' includes what was on his belt or on his animal. Anything else that is with his servant or in his house or tent is not salb. It is unrestricted, so it includes if the salb was with the polytheist as a loan from a child or woman, because their property is taken as booty like that of an adult. And if the salb was the property of a Muslim who entered their land with aman and the slain polytheist seized it, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, cutting off the Muslim's ownership. If the polytheists took the salb of the slain and then were defeated, it is booty and the killer gets nothing, because they owned it by seizure, so the killer's ownership is nullified, then the warriors own it, even if they don't know that they took it. If it was stripped from him, it is fay’ because they established their hand on it by stripping; otherwise it is for the killer. If the polytheists dragged or carried him on his animal with his weapons on it, it is different from if they carried their own weapons and belongings on it, which would be fay’. If something is found on an animal after the army has traveled one or two stages, and it is unknown whether anyone had it in hand or not, it belongs to the killer by qiyas, not istihsan. If someone says 'whoever kills a slain, he gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier while his servant has his horse standing near him between the ranks, the killer gets the horse if the horse was near his servant, because the Imam's intent is to kill one who is capable of fighting as a horseman, and this is so. But if it was not near him in the ranks, he does not get it. If he kills a polytheist on a baridhun (strong horse), he gets it because he is called a horseman. If he was on a donkey, mule, or ram, he does not deserve the salb because the rider of these things is not a horseman and thus does not deserve the horseman's share. In al-Muhit: thus it is known that what the commentator mentioned from al-Muhit that the Imam said 'whoever kills a slain gets his salb' is a slip of the pen; what is mentioned in al-Muhit is 'gets his horse'. Evidence is that the weapons should be sold from them. And the statement of the text in al-Ayman: 'whoever swears to commit a sin should break his oath' is common in their speech. His statement: (slip of the pen) – al-Ramli said: 'from some scribes, and in our copies from al-Zayla'i it is 'gets his horse' as in al-Muhit.' Narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Muwatta', Ahmad... He said further: if he was riding a mule or similar, he does not get it; but if the grant (tanfil) was worded as 'salb', he would deserve it because 'mount' (markab) is more general than horse. Al-Qamus says: al-markab (like masjad) refers to one of the mounts of land and sea. In al-Hidayah: then the ruling of tanfil is that it cuts off the right of others. As for ownership, it is only established after transport to dar al-Islam, as previously mentioned. So if the amir says 'whoever gets a slave girl, she is his', and a Muslim gets her and then performs istibra', he may not have intercourse with her, nor sell her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said he may have intercourse and sell her because, according to him, tanfil establishes ownership, just as ownership is established by division in dar al-harb and purchase from a harbi, and the obligation of guarantee for destruction is said to differ based on this difference. Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the rules of al-salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy) and al-tanfil (extra grants by the Imam). The commentator begins by citing al-Dhakhira that there is no prohibition (apparently referring to a previous issue). The main ruling: the salb belongs to the entire army, not exclusively to the killer, because it is obtained through the collective strength of the army, thus it is ghanimah divided among all. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet told Habib ibn Abi Salamah that he only gets what the Imam gives him. The hadith 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' is interpreted as referring to tanfil (a discretionary grant), not as a fixed right, to reconcile with the first hadith. The commentator then defines salb: according to custom, it includes the slain's mount, clothes, weapons, and what he carries. He cites various lexicons: al-Mughrib, al-Layth, al-Azhari, and al-Qamus. He elaborates: the mount includes its saddle and equipment; 'what is with the slain' includes what is on his belt or animal. Anything with his servant, in his house, or tent is not salb. The rule applies whether the salb was borrowed from a child or woman, as their property is also booty. If the salb was originally a Muslim's property seized by the slain polytheist, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, so the killer does not get it. If the polytheists take the salb from the slain and then flee, the salb becomes general booty, not for the killer, because the polytheists had owned it by seizure. If the polytheists stripped the slain of his salb, it becomes fay' (property of the state). If they dragged him or his animal, the rider's possessions are for the killer, but their own weapons on the animal are fay'. If a salb is found on an animal after the army has moved one or two stages, it goes to the killer by qiyas (analogy) but not by istihsan (juristic preference). The text then addresses a specific case: if the Imam says 'whoever kills gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier whose horse is nearby with his servant, the killer gets the horse if it is close. If not, he doesn't. Killing a polytheist on a strong horse (bardhun) gives entitlement to the horse because he is a horseman; but on a donkey, mule, or ram, no. The commentator corrects a scribal error: 'salba' should be 'farasahu' (his horse) as in al-Muhit. The passage then discusses al-tanfil: it cuts off the right of others to the grant. Ownership under tanfil is only established after transport to dar al-Islam. There is an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say that if the Imam grants a slave girl, the recipient cannot have intercourse or sell her until after transport, because ownership is not yet complete. Muhammad says he can, as tanfil establishes immediate ownership in dar al-harb, similar to division or purchase.

Key Terms
الاستيلاء (al-istila'): Seizure that transfers ownership in dar al-harb.
الفيء (al-fay'): Property taken from the enemy without force, or when polytheists strip salb, it becomes state treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If polytheists strip the slain of his armor (take it off his body), what is the ruling on that armor according to the passage?
A) It belongs to the killer.
B) It is divided as ghanimah.
C) It becomes fay' (state property).
D) It is returned to the Muslim who originally owned it.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of salb can be nullified or transformed by the polytheists' act of seizure (istila'), with degrees of possession determining the final category (ghanimah/fay'/killer's property).

The Horse Grant and the Condition of Horseman
This passage delves into a particular tanfil scenario and corrects a textual error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a killer is entitled to a slain's horse under a specific tanfil grant, based on whether the slain was considered a horseman.

Core Idea
A grant of 'the horse' only applies if the slain was a horseman; riders of donkeys or mules are excluded.

This lesson focuses on a specific scenario: the Imam says 'whoever kills a slain gets his horse.' Does this apply to any riding animal? The text clarifies that the term 'horse' (faras) is literal and tied to being a horseman (faris). The ruling depends on whether the slain was capable of fighting as a horseman. This involves distinguishing between types of mounts and the proximity of the horse to the slain. The passage also corrects a scribal error where 'salb' was mistakenly written instead of 'horse'.

Guided Path
1
State the Condition for the Horse Grant
If the Imam grants 'the horse' of the slain, the killer (even a foot soldier) can get it only if the slain was a horseman (rode a horse).
The grant is specific to the horse, not the salb in general.
2
Assess the Slain's Mount
If the slain rode a horse or a strong horse (bardhun), he is a horseman. But if he rode a donkey, mule, or ram, he is not, and the grant does not apply.
The term 'horse' is literal, not generic for any animal.
3
Consider the Proximity of the Horse
If the slain's horse was nearby (e.g., with his servant between the ranks), the killer gets it. If it was far away, he does not.
The intent is to reward the killing of a combatant horseman.
4
Correct the Scribal Error
The commentator notes that a previous version mistakenly wrote 'salb' but the correct word in al-Muhit is 'horse' (farasuhu). Evidence: weapons are sold separately, indicating the grant is for the horse only.
This shows the importance of textual verification.

ظاهر ما في الذخيرة عدم الحرمة قوله : (والسلب للكل إن لم ينفل) أي لا يختص به القاتل عندنا لأنه مأخوذ بقوة الجيش فيكون غنيمة فيقسم بينهم قسمة الغنائم كما نطق به النص وقال عليه السلام الحبيب بن أبي سلمة ليس لك من سلب قتيلك إلا ما طابت به نفس إمامك. وأما قوله عليه السلام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه» (۱) فيحتمل نصب الشرع ويحتمل التنفيل فنحمله على الثاني لما روينا قوله : (وهو) مركبه وثيابه وسلاحه وما معه) أي السلب ما ذكر للعرف. وفي المغرب : السلب المسلوب وعن الليث والأزهري : كل ما على الإنسان من اللباس فهو سلب وللفقهاء فيه كلام .اهـ. وفي القاموس السلب بالتحريك ما يسلب وجمعه أسلاب ودخل في مركبه ما كان عليه من سرج وآلة وما مع المقتول شامل لما كان في وسطه أو على دابته وما عدا ذلك مما هو مع غلامه أو في بيته أو في خيمته فليس بسلب. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان السلب عند المشرك عارية من صبي أو امرأة لأنه يستغنم مالهما كمال البالغ، وما إذا كان السلب ملكاً لمسلم دخل دارهم بأمان فغصبه المشرك المقتول لأنه ملكه بالاستيلاء فانقطع ملك المسلم عنه . ولو أخذ المشركون سلب المقتول ثم انهزموا فهو غنيمة ولا شيء للقاتل لأنهم ملكوه بالاستيلاء فبطل ملك القاتل ثم ملكه الغزاة وإن لم يدر أنهم أخذوه، فإن كان منزوعاً عنه فهو فيء لإثبات يدهم عليه بالنزع وإلا فهو للقاتل وإن جره المشركون أو حملوه على دابته وعليها سلاحه بخلاف ما إذا حملوا أسلحتهم وأمتعتهم عليها فإنه فيء. ولو وجد على دابة بعدما سار العسكر مرحلة أو مرحلتين ولا يدري أكان أحد في يد أو لا فهو للقاتل قياساً لا استحساناً ولو قال من قتل قتيلاً فله فرسه فقتل راجل راجلاً ومع غلامه فرسه قائم بجنبه بين الصفين يكون للقاتل فرسه إذا كان فرسه مع . غلامه بقرب منه لأن مقصود الإمام قتل من كان متمكناً من القتال فارساً وهذا كذلك، وإن لم يكن بجنبه في الصف فلا يكون له. ولو قتل مشركاً على برذون كان له لأنه يسمى فارساً، ولو كان على حمار أو بغل أو حمل لا يستحق السلب لأن راكب هذه الأشياء لا فارساً ولذا لا يسمى يستحق سهم الفارس؛ كذا في المحيط وبه علم أن ما ذكره الشارح عن المحيط بأنه قال الإمام من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه سبق قلم، وإنما المذكور في المحيط فله فرسه والدليل عليه أنه ينبغي أن يباع السلاح منهم وقول المتن في الأيمان ومن حلف على معصية ينبغي أن يحنث) شائع في كلامهم قوله : (سبق (قلم قال الرملي : أي من بعض النساخ والذي في نسخنا من الزيلعي فله فرسه كما في المحيط (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب المفازي باب .٥٤ مسلم في كتاب الجهاد حديث ٤٢. أبو داود في كتاب الجهاد باب ١٣٦. الترمذي في كتاب السير باب ۱۳ . الموطأ في كتاب الجهاد حديث ١٨. أحمد في مسنده (١٢/٥، ٢٩٥) قال آخراً: لو كان راكباً على بغل ونحوه لا يكون له ولو كان التنفيل بلفظ السلب لاستحقه لأن المركب أعم منه ومن الفرس. قال في القاموس : المركب كمقعد واحد مراكب البر والبحر اهـ. وفي الهداية ثم حكم التنفيل قطع حق الباقين فأما الملك فإنما يثبت بعد الإحراز بدار الإسلام لما مر من قبل حتى لو قال الأمير من أصاب جارية فهي له فأصابها مسلم فاستبرأها لم يجز له وطؤها وكذا لا يبيعها هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد له أن يطاها ويبيعها لأن التنفيل يثبت به الملك عنده كما يثبت بالقسمة في دار الحرب والشراء من الحربي ووجوب الضمان بالإتلاف قد قيل على هذا الاختلاف اهـ. والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم .
That which is apparent in al-Dhakhira is that there is no prohibition. His statement: (And the spoils (salb) belong to all if he did not grant (tanfil)) meaning the killer is not exclusively entitled to it according to us because it is taken by the strength of the army, so it becomes booty (ghanimah) and is divided among them like the division of booty, as the text (Quran) declares. And the Prophet ﷺ said to Habib ibn Abi Salamah: 'You have nothing from the spoils of your slain except what your Imam willingly gives you.' As for his statement ﷺ: 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' – it could mean a prescribed share (nasb al-shar') or it could mean an extra grant (tanfil). We interpret it as the second based on what we narrated. His statement: (And it is his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him) i.e., al-salb is what was mentioned according to custom. In al-Mughrib: al-salb is the plundered goods. From al-Layth and al-Azhari: everything on a person of clothing is salb, but jurists have discussion about it. In al-Qamus: al-salb (with fath of the letter sin) is what is plundered, plural aslāb. Included in 'his mount' is what is on it of saddle and equipment, and 'what is with the slain' includes what was on his belt or on his animal. Anything else that is with his servant or in his house or tent is not salb. It is unrestricted, so it includes if the salb was with the polytheist as a loan from a child or woman, because their property is taken as booty like that of an adult. And if the salb was the property of a Muslim who entered their land with aman and the slain polytheist seized it, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, cutting off the Muslim's ownership. If the polytheists took the salb of the slain and then were defeated, it is booty and the killer gets nothing, because they owned it by seizure, so the killer's ownership is nullified, then the warriors own it, even if they don't know that they took it. If it was stripped from him, it is fay’ because they established their hand on it by stripping; otherwise it is for the killer. If the polytheists dragged or carried him on his animal with his weapons on it, it is different from if they carried their own weapons and belongings on it, which would be fay’. If something is found on an animal after the army has traveled one or two stages, and it is unknown whether anyone had it in hand or not, it belongs to the killer by qiyas, not istihsan. If someone says 'whoever kills a slain, he gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier while his servant has his horse standing near him between the ranks, the killer gets the horse if the horse was near his servant, because the Imam's intent is to kill one who is capable of fighting as a horseman, and this is so. But if it was not near him in the ranks, he does not get it. If he kills a polytheist on a baridhun (strong horse), he gets it because he is called a horseman. If he was on a donkey, mule, or ram, he does not deserve the salb because the rider of these things is not a horseman and thus does not deserve the horseman's share. In al-Muhit: thus it is known that what the commentator mentioned from al-Muhit that the Imam said 'whoever kills a slain gets his salb' is a slip of the pen; what is mentioned in al-Muhit is 'gets his horse'. Evidence is that the weapons should be sold from them. And the statement of the text in al-Ayman: 'whoever swears to commit a sin should break his oath' is common in their speech. His statement: (slip of the pen) – al-Ramli said: 'from some scribes, and in our copies from al-Zayla'i it is 'gets his horse' as in al-Muhit.' Narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Muwatta', Ahmad... He said further: if he was riding a mule or similar, he does not get it; but if the grant (tanfil) was worded as 'salb', he would deserve it because 'mount' (markab) is more general than horse. Al-Qamus says: al-markab (like masjad) refers to one of the mounts of land and sea. In al-Hidayah: then the ruling of tanfil is that it cuts off the right of others. As for ownership, it is only established after transport to dar al-Islam, as previously mentioned. So if the amir says 'whoever gets a slave girl, she is his', and a Muslim gets her and then performs istibra', he may not have intercourse with her, nor sell her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said he may have intercourse and sell her because, according to him, tanfil establishes ownership, just as ownership is established by division in dar al-harb and purchase from a harbi, and the obligation of guarantee for destruction is said to differ based on this difference. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the rules of al-salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy) and al-tanfil (extra grants by the Imam). The commentator begins by citing al-Dhakhira that there is no prohibition (apparently referring to a previous issue). The main ruling: the salb belongs to the entire army, not exclusively to the killer, because it is obtained through the collective strength of the army, thus it is ghanimah divided among all. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet told Habib ibn Abi Salamah that he only gets what the Imam gives him. The hadith 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' is interpreted as referring to tanfil (a discretionary grant), not as a fixed right, to reconcile with the first hadith. The commentator then defines salb: according to custom, it includes the slain's mount, clothes, weapons, and what he carries. He cites various lexicons: al-Mughrib, al-Layth, al-Azhari, and al-Qamus. He elaborates: the mount includes its saddle and equipment; 'what is with the slain' includes what is on his belt or animal. Anything with his servant, in his house, or tent is not salb. The rule applies whether the salb was borrowed from a child or woman, as their property is also booty. If the salb was originally a Muslim's property seized by the slain polytheist, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, so the killer does not get it. If the polytheists take the salb from the slain and then flee, the salb becomes general booty, not for the killer, because the polytheists had owned it by seizure. If the polytheists stripped the slain of his salb, it becomes fay' (property of the state). If they dragged him or his animal, the rider's possessions are for the killer, but their own weapons on the animal are fay'. If a salb is found on an animal after the army has moved one or two stages, it goes to the killer by qiyas (analogy) but not by istihsan (juristic preference). The text then addresses a specific case: if the Imam says 'whoever kills gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier whose horse is nearby with his servant, the killer gets the horse if it is close. If not, he doesn't. Killing a polytheist on a strong horse (bardhun) gives entitlement to the horse because he is a horseman; but on a donkey, mule, or ram, no. The commentator corrects a scribal error: 'salba' should be 'farasahu' (his horse) as in al-Muhit. The passage then discusses al-tanfil: it cuts off the right of others to the grant. Ownership under tanfil is only established after transport to dar al-Islam. There is an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say that if the Imam grants a slave girl, the recipient cannot have intercourse or sell her until after transport, because ownership is not yet complete. Muhammad says he can, as tanfil establishes immediate ownership in dar al-harb, similar to division or purchase.

Key Terms
الفرس (al-faras): Horse; specifically the animal ridden in battle.
البرذون (al-bardhun): A strong horse (packhorse or steed).
سبق قلم (sabq qalam): Slip of the pen; a scribal error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the Imam grants the slain's horse, the killer gets the horse only if the slain is riding what type of animal?
A) Any riding animal.
B) A horse or a bardhun.
C) A horse only, not a bardhun.
D) Any animal that is considered a mount (markab).

Key Takeaway
A tanfil grant of 'the horse' applies only if the slain was a horseman (riding a horse or bardhun); the horse must also be nearby.

Tanfil and Ownership: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Immediate Possession
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute on when tanfil confers full ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf's view versus Muhammad's on when ownership is established under tanfil, and evaluate the practical effects.

Core Idea
Tanfil cuts off others' rights, but according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, full ownership only after transport to dar al-Islam.

The final section of the passage addresses a critical point: when does tanfil actually transfer ownership? While it is agreed that tanfil removes the right of other soldiers to the granted item, the timing of the grantee's full ownership is disputed within the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say ownership is not complete until the item is transported to dar al-Islam (thus no intercourse or sale before that). Muhammad argues that tanfil grants immediate ownership, like division in dar al-harb or purchase from a harbi. This lesson dissects this ikhtilaf and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Nature of Tanfil
The text says: 'the ruling of tanfil is that it cuts off the right of others.' This means the grantee's share is secured against other claimants.
Tanfil is not a division of existing property but a special grant.
2
Identify the Point of Dispute
The dispute is not about cutting off rights, but about when ownership (milk) is established: immediately in dar al-harb or only after transport (ihraz) to dar al-Islam.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
State the Position of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf
They say ownership is only after transport. Until then, the grantee cannot sell the item or (if a slave girl) have intercourse with her, even after she is cleared from pregnancy (istibra).
This is the cautious view, linking to the general rule that ghanimah ownership is contingent on transport.
4
State Muhammad's Position
Muhammad says immediate ownership, just like in division or purchase in dar al-harb. So the grantee can sell and have intercourse.
This gives more immediate benefit to the warrior.
5
Consider the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The difference affects whether the grantee can use the property freely before returning to Muslim territory.
The text does not give a tarjih, ending with 'Allah knows best'.

الترمذي في كتاب السير باب ۱۳ . الموطأ في كتاب الجهاد حديث ١٨. أحمد في مسنده (١٢/٥، ٢٩٥) قال آخراً: لو كان راكباً على بغل ونحوه لا يكون له ولو كان التنفيل بلفظ السلب لاستحقه لأن المركب أعم منه ومن الفرس. قال في القاموس : المركب كمقعد واحد مراكب البر والبحر اهـ. وفي الهداية ثم حكم التنفيل قطع حق الباقين فأما الملك فإنما يثبت بعد الإحراز بدار الإسلام لما مر من قبل حتى لو قال الأمير من أصاب جارية فهي له فأصابها مسلم فاستبرأها لم يجز له وطؤها وكذا لا يبيعها هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد له أن يطاها ويبيعها لأن التنفيل يثبت به الملك عنده كما يثبت بالقسمة في دار الحرب والشراء من الحربي ووجوب الضمان بالإتلاف قد قيل على هذا الاختلاف اهـ. والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم .
That which is apparent in al-Dhakhira is that there is no prohibition. His statement: (And the spoils (salb) belong to all if he did not grant (tanfil)) meaning the killer is not exclusively entitled to it according to us because it is taken by the strength of the army, so it becomes booty (ghanimah) and is divided among them like the division of booty, as the text (Quran) declares. And the Prophet ﷺ said to Habib ibn Abi Salamah: 'You have nothing from the spoils of your slain except what your Imam willingly gives you.' As for his statement ﷺ: 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' – it could mean a prescribed share (nasb al-shar') or it could mean an extra grant (tanfil). We interpret it as the second based on what we narrated. His statement: (And it is his mount, clothes, weapon, and what is with him) i.e., al-salb is what was mentioned according to custom. In al-Mughrib: al-salb is the plundered goods. From al-Layth and al-Azhari: everything on a person of clothing is salb, but jurists have discussion about it. In al-Qamus: al-salb (with fath of the letter sin) is what is plundered, plural aslāb. Included in 'his mount' is what is on it of saddle and equipment, and 'what is with the slain' includes what was on his belt or on his animal. Anything else that is with his servant or in his house or tent is not salb. It is unrestricted, so it includes if the salb was with the polytheist as a loan from a child or woman, because their property is taken as booty like that of an adult. And if the salb was the property of a Muslim who entered their land with aman and the slain polytheist seized it, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, cutting off the Muslim's ownership. If the polytheists took the salb of the slain and then were defeated, it is booty and the killer gets nothing, because they owned it by seizure, so the killer's ownership is nullified, then the warriors own it, even if they don't know that they took it. If it was stripped from him, it is fay’ because they established their hand on it by stripping; otherwise it is for the killer. If the polytheists dragged or carried him on his animal with his weapons on it, it is different from if they carried their own weapons and belongings on it, which would be fay’. If something is found on an animal after the army has traveled one or two stages, and it is unknown whether anyone had it in hand or not, it belongs to the killer by qiyas, not istihsan. If someone says 'whoever kills a slain, he gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier while his servant has his horse standing near him between the ranks, the killer gets the horse if the horse was near his servant, because the Imam's intent is to kill one who is capable of fighting as a horseman, and this is so. But if it was not near him in the ranks, he does not get it. If he kills a polytheist on a baridhun (strong horse), he gets it because he is called a horseman. If he was on a donkey, mule, or ram, he does not deserve the salb because the rider of these things is not a horseman and thus does not deserve the horseman's share. In al-Muhit: thus it is known that what the commentator mentioned from al-Muhit that the Imam said 'whoever kills a slain gets his salb' is a slip of the pen; what is mentioned in al-Muhit is 'gets his horse'. Evidence is that the weapons should be sold from them. And the statement of the text in al-Ayman: 'whoever swears to commit a sin should break his oath' is common in their speech. His statement: (slip of the pen) – al-Ramli said: 'from some scribes, and in our copies from al-Zayla'i it is 'gets his horse' as in al-Muhit.' Narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Muwatta', Ahmad... He said further: if he was riding a mule or similar, he does not get it; but if the grant (tanfil) was worded as 'salb', he would deserve it because 'mount' (markab) is more general than horse. Al-Qamus says: al-markab (like masjad) refers to one of the mounts of land and sea. In al-Hidayah: then the ruling of tanfil is that it cuts off the right of others. As for ownership, it is only established after transport to dar al-Islam, as previously mentioned. So if the amir says 'whoever gets a slave girl, she is his', and a Muslim gets her and then performs istibra', he may not have intercourse with her, nor sell her according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said he may have intercourse and sell her because, according to him, tanfil establishes ownership, just as ownership is established by division in dar al-harb and purchase from a harbi, and the obligation of guarantee for destruction is said to differ based on this difference. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the rules of al-salb (spoils taken from a slain enemy) and al-tanfil (extra grants by the Imam). The commentator begins by citing al-Dhakhira that there is no prohibition (apparently referring to a previous issue). The main ruling: the salb belongs to the entire army, not exclusively to the killer, because it is obtained through the collective strength of the army, thus it is ghanimah divided among all. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet told Habib ibn Abi Salamah that he only gets what the Imam gives him. The hadith 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils' is interpreted as referring to tanfil (a discretionary grant), not as a fixed right, to reconcile with the first hadith. The commentator then defines salb: according to custom, it includes the slain's mount, clothes, weapons, and what he carries. He cites various lexicons: al-Mughrib, al-Layth, al-Azhari, and al-Qamus. He elaborates: the mount includes its saddle and equipment; 'what is with the slain' includes what is on his belt or animal. Anything with his servant, in his house, or tent is not salb. The rule applies whether the salb was borrowed from a child or woman, as their property is also booty. If the salb was originally a Muslim's property seized by the slain polytheist, it becomes the polytheist's property by seizure, so the killer does not get it. If the polytheists take the salb from the slain and then flee, the salb becomes general booty, not for the killer, because the polytheists had owned it by seizure. If the polytheists stripped the slain of his salb, it becomes fay' (property of the state). If they dragged him or his animal, the rider's possessions are for the killer, but their own weapons on the animal are fay'. If a salb is found on an animal after the army has moved one or two stages, it goes to the killer by qiyas (analogy) but not by istihsan (juristic preference). The text then addresses a specific case: if the Imam says 'whoever kills gets his horse', and a foot soldier kills a foot soldier whose horse is nearby with his servant, the killer gets the horse if it is close. If not, he doesn't. Killing a polytheist on a strong horse (bardhun) gives entitlement to the horse because he is a horseman; but on a donkey, mule, or ram, no. The commentator corrects a scribal error: 'salba' should be 'farasahu' (his horse) as in al-Muhit. The passage then discusses al-tanfil: it cuts off the right of others to the grant. Ownership under tanfil is only established after transport to dar al-Islam. There is an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say that if the Imam grants a slave girl, the recipient cannot have intercourse or sell her until after transport, because ownership is not yet complete. Muhammad says he can, as tanfil establishes immediate ownership in dar al-harb, similar to division or purchase.

Key Terms
التنفيل (al-tanfil): Extra grant from the Imam to a warrior, cutting off others' rights to that item.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Transporting the spoils to dar al-Islam, a condition for complete ownership in ghanimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical consequence of the disagreement between Abu Hanifah/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a slave girl granted as tanfil?
A) Only Muhammad allows the killer to take the slave girl back home.
B) Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf forbid intercourse until after transport, while Muhammad allows it immediately after istibra'.
C) They disagree on whether istibra' is required.
D) They disagree on whether the killer can kill the slave girl's owner.

Key Takeaway
Tanfil establishes priority over others, but full ownership (including the right to sell or have intercourse with a slave girl) is only after transport according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad argues it is immediate.

Disbelievers' Seizure of Property: Ownership and Condition of Transport
Opening of chapter on seizure by disbelievers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership by disbelievers of property seized in dar al-harb and identify the condition of ihraz that validates that ownership.

Core Idea
Disbelievers own what they seize in dar al-harb; Muslims may take it lawfully if the property was transported there.

This passage opens a critical chapter in Hanafi fiqh: the status of property captured by disbelievers during warfare. Understanding when such property becomes owned by the captors is essential for determining its legality when Muslims later acquire it. The author establishes the principle that seizure in dar al-harb transfers ownership, with the key condition of ihraz (transport to dar al-harb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Extract the main claim: when disbelievers capture property from each other in dar al-harb, they own it. This is the starting point for later acquisition by Muslims.
The example: Turks capture Romans.
2
Examine the Legal Proof
The author analogizes (qiyas) this seizure to other properties of disbelievers in dar al-harb that are recognized as owned. This is a talil that uses their general ownership as an indicator.
The phrase 'اعتباراً بسائر أملاكهم' (by analogy to their other properties) is key.
3
Identify the Condition of Ihraz
Note that ownership only fully vests when the captured property is transported (ihraz) into dar al-harb. Without this, the original owner's right remains.
This is highlighted in the reference to al-Khulasah.
4
Apply to Special Scenarios
Work through the cases involving peace treaties and fighting in Muslim territory. This tests the limit of the principle: ihraz is absent when fighting occurs in dar al-Islam, so no ownership transfers.
Also consider the question about kinship and musta'min.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب استيلاء الكفار سبى الترك الروم وأخذوا أموالهم ملكوها وملكنا ما نجده من ذلك إن غلبنا عليهم اعتباراً بسائر أملاكهم. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان بيننا وبين الروم موادعة لأنا لم نغدرهم إنما أخذنا ما لا خرج عن ملكهم ولذا حل لنا أن نشتري ما غنمه إحدى الطائفتين من الأخرى لما ذكرنا وفي الخلاصة والإحراز بدار الحرب شرط أما بدارهم فلا ولو كان بيننا وبين كل من الطائفتين موادعة واقتتلوا في دارنا لا نشتري من الغالبين شيئاً لأنهم لم يملكوه لعدم الإحراز فيكون شراؤنا غدراً بالآخرين فإنه على ملكهم. وأما لو اقتتلت طائفتان في بلدة واحدة فهل يجوز شراء المسلم المستأمن من الغالبين نفساً أو ينبغي أن يقال: إن كان بين المأخوذ والآخذ قرابة محرمية كالأمية أو كان المأخوذ لا من مالاً؟
Chapter on the seizure by disbelievers. The Turks took the Romans captive and took their wealth; they owned it. And we own what we find thereof if we overcome them, analogously to their other properties. It is unrestricted, so it includes the case where there is a peace treaty between us and the Romans, because we did not betray them; we only took what left their ownership. Therefore it is permissible for us to purchase what one of the two parties looted from the other, as we mentioned. In al-Khulasah: and transport (ihraz) to dar al-harb is a condition; as for their land, it is not. If there is a peace treaty between us and both parties, and they fight in our land, we do not purchase anything from the victors because they did not own it due to lack of transport (ihraz); thus our purchase would be treachery against the others, as it remains their property. As for if two parties fight in one town, is it permissible for a Muslim musta'min to purchase from the victors? Or should it be said: if there is a prohibited degree of kinship between the taken and the taker, like the mother, or if the taken has no wealth?
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on seizure by disbelievers (istila' al-kuffar). It establishes a key principle: when disbelievers (here exemplified by Turks and Romans) capture each other's property in dar al-harb, they own it. That ownership is recognized by Islamic law. Consequently, when Muslims later defeat them and seize that property, it becomes lawful for Muslims. The reasoning is that the disbelievers' ownership is valid in their own abode, analogous to their other lawful properties. The commentator notes that this applies even if there is a peace treaty (muwada'ah) between Muslims and the Romans, because the property has left the original owners' dominion; there is no treachery in taking it. Therefore, Muslims may purchase such goods from the victors. However, there is a condition: the property must have been transported (ihraz) to dar al-harb by the victors; if it is still in dar al-Islam or in a place where ihraz is not achieved, ownership is not established. For instance, if both parties are under a peace treaty with Muslims and they fight in Muslim territory, the victors do not own the spoils because there is no ihraz; buying from them would be treachery towards the other party, as their property right remains intact. The passage ends with a question: if two groups fight within a single town, can a Muslim musta'min buy from the victors? The answer is conditional on factors like kinship (e.g., mahram) or the captured person having no wealth. The text is cut off, but the implication is that there are exceptions.

Key Terms
استيلاء (istila'): Seizure or taking control by force, specifically by disbelievers over property in dar al-harb.
الإحراز (al-ihraz): Transporting captured property into one's own territory (dar al-harb or dar al-Islam), which is a condition for ownership.
موادعة (muwada'ah): A peace treaty or truce between Muslims and disbelievers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if Turks capture goods from Romans in dar al-harb, and then Muslims defeat the Turks and take those goods, are the goods lawful for the Muslims?
A) No, because the Turks had no valid ownership.
B) Yes, because the Turks owned them by seizure, and Muslims can take them.
C) Only if the goods were not originally Muslim property.
D) No, because there was a peace treaty.

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers own property seized in dar al-harb if transported there; Muslims may lawfully acquire it when they overcome them.

Recovery by Lessee, Depositary, and Borrower
Rulings on lessee, depositary, and borrower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rights of a lessee, depositary, and borrower in reclaiming captured property before and after division of spoils.

Core Idea
Before division, possessors reclaim without payment; after division, only the lessee may reclaim by paying value.

In Hanafi law, a person who has possession of property (e.g., as lessee, depositary, or borrower) has certain rights to reclaim that property if it is captured by enemies and becomes part of war booty. This lesson examines the differences in their rights before and after the spoils are divided among the warriors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of possessor
Determine whether the claimant is a lessee, depositary, or borrower of the captured property. Each has a different legal standing.
The rights of a lessee are broader than those of a depositary or borrower.
2
Determine the stage of division
Check whether the spoils have been divided (qismah) or not. This determines which rule applies.
Before division, the property is still in the common pool of booty.
3
Apply the ruling before division
If before division, the lessee, depositary, and borrower may all litigate and take the property without any payment.
4
Apply the ruling after division
If after division, only the lessee may reclaim the property by paying its value. The depositary and borrower have no such right; they are like strangers.
The lessee can also bring evidence of the lease if the new owner denies it.
5
Understand the consequences of reclamation
When the lessee takes the property, the lease revives and the rent for the captivity period is waived. The property returns to its previous status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عينه وأخذ أرشه فإن تكرر الأسر والشراء أخذه الأول من الثاني بثمنه ثم القديم مع أن التي نقدها؛ كذا في التتارخانية أنه في الأخيرة مشكل لأنه بأردأ منه وصفاً فينبغي يكون للمالك الأخذ . وههنا مسائل لا بأس بإيرادها تكثيراً للفوائد منها : أن العين المحرزة لو كانت في يد مستأجر أو مودع أو مستعير هل له المخاصمة والاسترداد أم لا؟ قالوا : للمستأجر أن يخاصم في المغنوم ويأخذه قبل القسمة بغير شيء، وكذا المستعير والمستودع، وإذا أخذه المستأجر عاد العبد إلى الإجارة وسقط عنه الأجر في مدة أسره، وإن كان بعد القسمة فللمستأجر أخذه بالقيمة، فإن أنكر الذي وقع في سهمه الإجارة فأقام المستأجر البينة قبلت بينته وثبتت الإجارة، وليس للمستعير والمستودع المخاصمة بعد القسمة فكانا بمنزلة الأجنبي. ومنها لو وهبها العدو لمسلم فأخرجها إلى دار الإسلام أخذها المالك بقيمته لأنه ثبت له ملك خاص فلا يزال إلا بالقيمة ومنها لو أسر العدو الجارية المبيعة قبل القبض ونقد الثمن ثم اشتراها رجل منهم يأخذها البائع بالثمن ولا يكون متطوعاً لأنه يحيى به حقه فيرجع به على المشتري والثمن الثاني واجب على المشتري الثاني بعقده ومنها إذا وقع العبد المأسور في سهم رجل فدبره أو أعتقه جاز ولا يبقى للمولى عليه سبيل لأن المأسور منه لا يملك نقض تصرف المالك في المأسور. ولو زوجها وولدت من الزوج له أخذها وولدها لأن التزويج لا يمنع النفل ولا يفسخ النكاح، وإن أخذ عقرها أو أرش جناية عليها ليس للمولى عليها سبيل لأن الولد من أجزائها وهي كانت ملكاً له والعقر والأرش لم يكن من أجزائها وإنما وجب في ملك مستأنف للمشتري، ولأنهما من ذوات الأمثال فلا تجري فيهما المفاداة لأنها لا تفيد. ومنها أن للوصي أن يأخذ المأسور لليتيم من مشتريه بالثمن ولا يأخذه لنفسه بشرط أن يكون الثمن مثل قيمته. ومنها لو رهنه المشتري فليس لمولاه عليه سبيل حتى يفتكه ولا يجبر على الافتكاك إلا أن يتطوع بأداء الدين ثم يعطى الثمن فله ذلك بخلاف ما إذا آجره المشتري فللمولى أخذه وإبطال الإجارة لأنها تنفسح بالأعذار وهذا عذر بخلاف الرهن. ومنها لو أسروا عبداً في عنقه جناية أو دين فرجع إلى مولاه القديم فالكل في رقبته، وإن لم يرجع إليه أو رجع بملك مبتدأ فجناية العمد والدين بحاله وسقطت جناية الخطأ لأن العمد متعلق بروحه والدين بذمته، وأما الخطأ فمتعلق بماليته ابتداء فإذا خرج عن ملك المولى إلى ملك لا يخلفه بطل الكل كما في المحيط .
His eye and he took its compensation. If capture and purchase are repeated, the first takes from the second for his price, then the old owner with two prices... and here are issues: the secured chattel if in the hand of a lessee, depositary, or borrower, does he have the right to litigate and reclaim? They said: the lessee may litigate regarding the spoils and take it before division without anything, likewise the borrower and depositary. When the lessee takes it, the slave returns to the lease and the rent falls during his captivity. If after division, the lessee takes it for its value. If the one in whose share it fell denies the lease, and the lessee brings evidence, his evidence is accepted and the lease is established. The borrower and depositary have no litigation after division, they are like a stranger. Among them: if the enemy gifted it to a Muslim and he brought it to Dar al-Islam, the owner takes it for its value because a specific ownership was established, so it is not removed except for value. Among them: if the enemy captured a sold slave girl before delivery and the price was paid, then a man from them bought her, the seller takes her for the price and is not a volunteer because he revives his right, so he recovers from the buyer, and the second price is obligatory on the second buyer by his contract. Among them: if the captured slave falls into the share of a man and he makes him mudabbar or sets him free, it is valid and the former master has no recourse because the captive does not have the power to invalidate the owner's disposition over the captive. If he marries her and she gives birth from the husband, he may take her and her offspring because marriage does not prevent taking and does not annul the marriage. If he takes her bride-price or compensation for injury on her, the master has no recourse because the offspring is part of her and she was his property, but the bride-price and compensation were not part of her; they were obligated in a new ownership of the buyer. And because they are of the type of the like, redemption does not apply to them because it is not beneficial. Among them: the guardian may take the captive for the orphan from his buyer for the price, but he may not take it for himself on condition that the price equals its value. Among them: if the buyer pledges it, the master has no recourse until he ransoms it, and he is not compelled to ransom unless he volunteers to pay the debt then he is given the price, so he has that, contrary to if the buyer leases it, the master may take it and invalidate the lease because leases are dissolved by excuses and this is an excuse, unlike the pledge. Among them: if they capture a slave with a jinaya or debt on his neck, and he returns to his old master, then everything is on his neck. If he does not return to him or returns with a new ownership, then intentional jinaya and debt remain as they were, and unintentional jinaya is dropped because intentional is related to his life and debt to his liability, while unintentional is initially related to his wealth, so when he leaves the ownership of the master to an ownership that does not succeed it, everything is void as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (masa'il) regarding the recovery of captured property from various possessors. The commentator lists several scenarios: (1) A lessee, depositary, or borrower of a captured slave can litigate and reclaim before division without payment. After division, only the lessee can reclaim by paying the value, and his evidence of lease is accepted. (2) If the enemy gives the captured property to a Muslim as a gift, the original owner can reclaim it by paying its value. (3) If a sold slave girl is captured before delivery and the buyer already paid, and then an enemy buys her, the original seller can take her from that buyer for the price he paid. (4) If a captured slave falls to a warrior's share and he frees him or makes him mudabbar, it is valid and the original owner has no recourse. (5) If the warrior marries a captured slave girl and she has a child, the original owner can take both the mother and child, but cannot take the bride-price or compensation for injury. (6) A guardian may redeem a captive for an orphan but not for himself. (7) If the buyer pledges the captive, the original owner cannot take it until the pledge is redeemed, but if the buyer leases it, the owner can take it and cancel the lease. (8) If a slave is captured with a debt or jinaya, the implications differ based on whether he returns to the original owner or is purchased anew. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi position on the rights of original owners versus subsequent possessors in the context of war booty.

Key Terms
العين المحرزة (al-ayn al-muhrazah): The secured chattel; property that is in the possession of someone (lessee, depositary, etc.)
المخاصمة (al-mukhasamah): Litigation or legal claim to recover the property
القسمة (al-qismah): Division of spoils among the warriors

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a lessee's slave is captured and then sold to a buyer, can the lessee reclaim the slave after division?
A) Yes, without payment.
B) Yes, by paying the value.
C) No, only the owner can reclaim.
D) No, the lessee has no right after division.

Key Takeaway
Before division, lessees, depositaries, and borrowers can reclaim captured property without payment; after division, only the lessee can reclaim by paying the value.

Rights of Original Owner and Other Claimants in Captured Property
Collection of miscellaneous rulings on recovery of captured property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the circumstances under which an original owner, a seller, a guardian, and others can reclaim captured property, and identify when payment is required or when the right is lost.

Core Idea
The original owner's right to reclaim captured property is not absolute; it depends on transfers, payments, and the nature of the property.

In addition to possessors, other parties have rights to recover captured property. This lesson covers scenarios such as the enemy gifting the property, a sold slave girl captured before delivery, a captive being freed or married, actions of a guardian, and the effect of debts and jinaya on the slave's neck.

Guided Path
1
Gift from the enemy
If the enemy gifts the captured property to a Muslim who brings it to Dar al-Islam, the original owner can reclaim it by paying its value, because a specific ownership was established.
The payment is required to remove the new owner's title.
2
Sold slave girl captured before delivery
If a sold slave girl is captured before delivery and the price was paid, and then an enemy buys her, the original seller can take her from that buyer for the price the buyer paid.
The seller is not a volunteer; he is reviving his right and can recover the price from the buyer.
3
Captive freed or made mudabbar
If a captive falls to a warrior's share and he frees him or makes him mudabbar, it is valid and the original owner has no recourse.
The captive cannot invalidate the new owner's disposition.
4
Marriage of captive and offspring
If the warrior marries a captured slave girl and she has a child, the original owner can take both mother and child, but cannot take the bride-price or compensation for injury on her.
The bride-price and compensation are not part of the original property.
5
Guardian's actions
A guardian may reclaim a captive for an orphan by paying the price, but cannot reclaim for himself unless he pays full value.
6
Pledge vs. lease
If the buyer pledges the captive, the original owner cannot take it until the pledge is redeemed. If the buyer leases it, the owner can take it and cancel the lease.
Leases are dissolved by this excuse, unlike pledges.
7
Slave with debt or jinaya
If a slave with a jinaya or debt is captured and returns to his old master, the debts remain. If he does not return, intentional jinaya and debt remain, but unintentional jinaya drops because it is related to wealth that is lost.

His eye and he took its compensation. If capture and purchase are repeated, the first takes from the second for his price, then the old owner with two prices... and here are issues: the secured chattel if in the hand of a lessee, depositary, or borrower, does he have the right to litigate and reclaim? They said: the lessee may litigate regarding the spoils and take it before division without anything, likewise the borrower and depositary. When the lessee takes it, the slave returns to the lease and the rent falls during his captivity. If after division, the lessee takes it for its value. If the one in whose share it fell denies the lease, and the lessee brings evidence, his evidence is accepted and the lease is established. The borrower and depositary have no litigation after division, they are like a stranger. Among them: if the enemy gifted it to a Muslim and he brought it to Dar al-Islam, the owner takes it for its value because a specific ownership was established, so it is not removed except for value. Among them: if the enemy captured a sold slave girl before delivery and the price was paid, then a man from them bought her, the seller takes her for the price and is not a volunteer because he revives his right, so he recovers from the buyer, and the second price is obligatory on the second buyer by his contract. Among them: if the captured slave falls into the share of a man and he makes him mudabbar or sets him free, it is valid and the former master has no recourse because the captive does not have the power to invalidate the owner's disposition over the captive. If he marries her and she gives birth from the husband, he may take her and her offspring because marriage does not prevent taking and does not annul the marriage. If he takes her bride-price or compensation for injury on her, the master has no recourse because the offspring is part of her and she was his property, but the bride-price and compensation were not part of her; they were obligated in a new ownership of the buyer. And because they are of the type of the like, redemption does not apply to them because it is not beneficial. Among them: the guardian may take the captive for the orphan from his buyer for the price, but he may not take it for himself on condition that the price equals its value. Among them: if the buyer pledges it, the master has no recourse until he ransoms it, and he is not compelled to ransom unless he volunteers to pay the debt then he is given the price, so he has that, contrary to if the buyer leases it, the master may take it and invalidate the lease because leases are dissolved by excuses and this is an excuse, unlike the pledge. Among them: if they capture a slave with a jinaya or debt on his neck, and he returns to his old master, then everything is on his neck. If he does not return to him or returns with a new ownership, then intentional jinaya and debt remain as they were, and unintentional jinaya is dropped because intentional is related to his life and debt to his liability, while unintentional is initially related to his wealth, so when he leaves the ownership of the master to an ownership that does not succeed it, everything is void as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (masa'il) regarding the recovery of captured property from various possessors. The commentator lists several scenarios: (1) A lessee, depositary, or borrower of a captured slave can litigate and reclaim before division without payment. After division, only the lessee can reclaim by paying the value, and his evidence of lease is accepted. (2) If the enemy gives the captured property to a Muslim as a gift, the original owner can reclaim it by paying its value. (3) If a sold slave girl is captured before delivery and the buyer already paid, and then an enemy buys her, the original seller can take her from that buyer for the price he paid. (4) If a captured slave falls to a warrior's share and he frees him or makes him mudabbar, it is valid and the original owner has no recourse. (5) If the warrior marries a captured slave girl and she has a child, the original owner can take both the mother and child, but cannot take the bride-price or compensation for injury. (6) A guardian may redeem a captive for an orphan but not for himself. (7) If the buyer pledges the captive, the original owner cannot take it until the pledge is redeemed, but if the buyer leases it, the owner can take it and cancel the lease. (8) If a slave is captured with a debt or jinaya, the implications differ based on whether he returns to the original owner or is purchased anew. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi position on the rights of original owners versus subsequent possessors in the context of war booty.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death
الجارية (al-jariyah): Female slave
العقر (al-'uqr): Bride-price or dower for a slave woman
أرش الجناية (arsh al-jinayah): Compensation for injury inflicted on a slave

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A captive slave is freed by the warrior in whose share he fell. Can the original owner reclaim him?
A) Yes, by paying compensation.
B) No, the freeing is valid and the owner's right is lost.
C) Yes, because the warrior is not the true owner.
D) No, but only if the freeing was done after division.

Key Takeaway
The rights to reclaim captured property vary by scenario: sometimes payment is required, sometimes rights are lost, and debts may survive or drop depending on the nature of the liability.
