Ruling on Blinding the Captive and Repeated Capture
Expansion on two rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the owner's option when a captive is blinded by the purchaser, and explain the sequence of rights in repeated capture.

Core Idea
The original owner may reclaim a blinded captive only in its current condition; in repeated captures, each buyer reclaims from the next.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two connected rulings from the Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The first concerns the effect of injury to a captive slave by the purchaser, and the second addresses the rights when a slave is captured multiple times. Both rulings illustrate the Hanafi principle that ownership rights are preserved but subject to the changes that occur during possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-issues
Locate the two distinct matn statements being commented on: (a) blinding the captive, (b) repeated capture. The commentary addresses each separately.
The matn phrases are: 'وإن فقأ عينيه وأخذ أرشه' and 'فإن تكرر الأسر والشراء أخذ الأول من الثاني بثمنه ثم القديم بالثمنين'.
2
State the ruling for blinding
The owner may take the slave from the purchaser by paying the price, but does not get the compensation (arsh) for the injury. The reason is that in a valid sale, attributes like eyesight are not separately priced.
This contrasts with shuf'a, where the sale is defective, so compensation is possible.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation
Abu Hanifa holds that the owner takes the slave in its blind state for its value as blind. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the owner takes it for its sound value, i.e., the compensation paid by the gouger. The difference hinges on whether the gouger's act is considered with or without the owner's implied consent.
The preferred view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's, as indicated by the commentator.
4
Explain the ruling for repeated capture
If a slave is captured and sold, then captured again and sold again, the first buyer has the right to take the slave from the second buyer by paying the price the second buyer paid. Then the original owner can take it from the first buyer by paying both prices. The original owner cannot take directly from the second buyer because his ownership was interrupted by the first capture.
This ensures that each buyer recovers his investment in order.
5
Identify the underlying principle
The commentary contrasts valid ownership (sale) with defective ownership (shuf'a) to justify why compensation is not transferred. In a valid sale, the owner is considered to have accepted the attribute's loss; in shuf'a, the buyer's possession is akin to a void contract, so attributes are guaranteed.
This principle also explains why the owner cannot claim the compensation from the gouger separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن فقأ عينيه وأخذ أرشه) أي للمالك أن يأخذه بالثمن من التاجر وإن خنزير منهم من أخذه بقيمة الخمر وإن شاء تركه انتهت وفي التتارخانية : ولو كان المشتري اشترى هذا الكر بخمر أو خنزير وأخرجه إلى دار الإسلام لم يكن للمالك القديم أن يأخذه على الروايات كلها اهـ. والذي يظهر أن المبيع إن كان مثلياً أخذه بقيمة الخمر وإن كان قيمياً فبقيمته نفسه. والأول محمل كلام الجوهرة، والثاني محمل كلام السراج ولا ينافيه ما في التتارخانية فتأمل وراجع.
بالثمنين ولا يملكون حرنا ومدبرنا وأم ولدنا ومكاتبنا ونملك عليهم جميع ذلك وإن ند كانت عينه فقئت وأخذ التاجر أرشها يعنى لا يحط شيئاً من الثمن ولا يأخذ المالك الأرش. أما الأول فلأن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن بخلاف الشفعة لأن الصفقة لما تحولت إلى الشفيع صار المشتري في يد المشتري بمنزلة المشتري شراء فاسداً والأوصاف تضمن فيه كما في الغصب، أما هنا الملك صحيح فافترقا وأما الثاني فلأن الملك فيه صحيح فلو أخذه أخذه بمثله وهو لا يفيد وظاهر ما في فتح القدير أن الفاقئ غير التاجر فإنه قال : ولو أنه فقأ عند الغازي المقسوم له فأخذ قيمته وسلمه للفاقئ فللمالك الأول أخذه من الفاقئ بقيمته أعمى عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا بقيمته سليماً وهي التي أعطاها الفاقئ للمولى. والفرق لأبي حنيفة أن فوات الطرف هنا بفعل الذي ملكه باختياره فكان بمنزلة ما لو اشتراه سليماً ثم قطع طرفه باختياره فكان راضياً بتنقيصه بخلاف مسألة الكتاب لأن الفاقئ غيره بغير رضاه اهـ . وصرح في المحيط بأن المشتري إذا فقأ عينها فالحكم كذلك. وعن محمد أنه تسقط حصته من الثمن، وهذا بمنزلة الشفعة إذا هدم المشتري البناء سقط عن الشفيع حصة البناء فكذا هذا اهـ. فعلى رواية محمد لا فرق بين مسألة الكتاب والشفعة إذ الوصف لا يقابله شيء إلا إذا صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف وهو موافق لما ذكروه في البيوع لكن ظاهر الهداية الفرق بين الكتاب والشفعة وهو الحق. ولا فرق في الفاقئ بين أن يكون التاجر أو غيره ولهذا قال الشارح: : الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في ملك صحيح بعد القبض وإن كانت مقصودة بالإتلاف بخلاف المشفوع لأن شراءه من غير رضا الشفيع مكروه وملكه ينتقض من غير رضاه فأشبه البيع الفاسد اهـ . ولو أخرجه المشتري من ملکه بعوض يأخذه العدو عن المالك القديم بذلك العوض إن كان مالاً، وإن كان غير مال كالصلح عن دم أو هبة أخذه بقيمته ولا ينتقض تصرفه بخلاف الشفيع لأن حقه قبل حق المشتري فينتقض تصرف المشتري لأجله، والتقييد بالعين اتفاقي لأن اليد لو قطعت فالحكم كذلك. ولو ولدت الجارية عند المشتري فأعتق المشتري أحدهما أخذ الباقي منهما بجميع الثمن لأن الفداء لا يتوزع ما بقي شيء من الأصل أو ما تولد منه، وعن محمد إن أعتق الأم أخذ الولد بحصته من الثمن وليس الولد كالأرش؛ كذا في المحيط . وفي المغرب : فقأ العين غارها بأن شق حدقتها، والقلع أن ينزع حدقتها بعروقها والأرش دية الجراحات والجمع أروش اهـ.
مسألة
قوله : ( فإن تكرر الأسر والشراء أخذ الأول من الثاني بثمنه ثم القديم بالثمنين) يعني لو أسر العبد مرتين واشتراه في المرأة الأولى رجل وفي الثانية رجر آخر كان حق الأخذ من المشتري الثاني للمشتري الأول بما اشترى لأن الأسر ورد على ملكة وأفاد أنه ليس للمالك القديم أن يأخذه من المشتري الثاني ولو كان المشتري الأول غائباً أو كان حاضراً إلا أنه أبى عن أخذه لأن الأسر ما ورد على ملكه فإذا أخذه المشتري الأول من الثاني بثمنه فقد قام
His statement: (And if he gouged out his eyes and took its compensation) means the owner may take it from the merchant for the price... (then the text continues with the matn: 'They do not possess our free, mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatab, and we possess all that from them...' meaning no reduction of price and the owner does not take the compensation. As for the first, because attributes are not compensated by any part of the price, unlike shuf'a because when the transaction shifts to the shufi', the buyer in the hands of the buyer becomes like a buyer in a void contract, and attributes are guaranteed as in usurpation. Here ownership is valid, so they differ. As for the second, because ownership is valid, if he takes it he takes it as its equivalent and that is not beneficial. And what is apparent in Fath al-Qadir is that the gouger is other than the merchant... and the difference for Abu Hanifa is that the loss of the limb here is by the act of the one who owned it by his choice, so it is like if he bought it sound then cut its limb by choice, so he was consenting to its diminishment, unlike the case of the text because the gouger is other than him without his consent. And from Muhammad: that his share of the price drops... Then the matn: 'If capture and purchase are repeated, the first takes from the second for his price, then the old owner by two prices' meaning if a slave is captured twice and bought in the first instance by a man and in the second by another man, the right of taking from the second buyer belongs to the first buyer for what he bought because capture occurred on his ownership, and it indicates that the old owner does not take from the second buyer even if the first buyer is absent or present but refuses to take, because capture did not occur on his ownership. So when the first buyer takes it from the second for his price, he has established...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage contains commentary on two matn statements. The first concerns the ruling when a captive slave has its eyes gouged out and the purchaser takes compensation. The commentator explains that the original owner can still take the slave by paying the price, but he does not get the compensation. The reason is that in a valid sale, attributes like eyesight are not part of the price, unlike in shuf'a where the sale is defective. An intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: Abu Hanifa holds that if the gouging is done by someone other than the purchaser, the owner can only take the blind slave for its value as blind, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the owner takes it for its sound value. The second matn deals with repeated capture and purchase: if a slave is captured and sold, then captured again and sold again, the first buyer has the right to take the slave from the second buyer by paying the price the second buyer paid, and then the original owner can take it from the first buyer by paying both prices. The explanation clarifies that the original owner cannot take directly from the second buyer because his ownership was interrupted by the first capture.

Key Terms
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation for injury; the monetary compensation paid for causing a wound or loss of a limb.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption right of a co-owner to purchase a sold share.
فقأ العين (faqa'a al-ayn): Gouging out the eye; blinding by piercing or removing the eyeball.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, if a captive slave is blinded by the purchaser, what can the original owner do?
A) Reclaim the slave for its sound value and also take the compensation.
B) Reclaim the slave for its blind value and not take the compensation.
C) Only take the compensation and not reclaim the slave.
D) He cannot reclaim because the purchaser owns it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the original owner of a captive may reclaim it in its damaged condition without compensation, and in repeated captures, each buyer reclaims in sequence.

Taking a Slave and Property from Harbi: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pricing of a slave vs. goods when redeemed from kuffar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding taking a slave and other property from harbi kuffar, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A slave captured by kuffar is taken free; other goods must be paid for—but Sahibayn disagree.

When Muslims redeem a Muslim slave captured by enemy non-Muslims along with other property, how are these items treated? The Hanafi school has an internal difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the disagreement and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the setting: kuffar (harbi) have seized a Muslim slave and other goods (e.g., a horse). A Muslim later buys the entire bundle from them. The question is what must be paid for the slave vs. the goods.
The slave is a Muslim; the goods are inanimate.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer takes the slave for free (majjanan) but must pay for the other goods with the price.
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) according to the commentary.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the buyer must pay for both the slave and the goods with the price.
They consider the bundle as a single transaction.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the slave's Islamic status (riqq) prevents the kuffar from owning him through domination (istila'), so their ownership is invalid. The Sahibayn argue that the state of togetherness (ijtima') should be treated like separate items, so each is paid for.
The key difference is whether riqq creates an exception.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the buyer gets the slave free; under Sahibayn, he must pay. The commentary leans towards Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position.
Mufta bihi: Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب استيلاء الكفار
أخذ العبد مجاناً وغيره بالثمن وإن ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم أو أمن عبد ثمة فجاءنا أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق .
فتح القدير. وفي شرح الوقاية الخلاف فيما إذا أخذوه قهراً وقيدوه، وأما إذا لم يكن قهراً فلا يملكونه اتفاقاً اهـ .
يعتق
قوله : ولو أبق بفرس أو متاع فاشترى رجل كله منهم أخذ العبد مجاناً وغيره بالثمن) يعني عند الإمام رضي الله عنه . وقالا : يأخذ العبد وما معه بالثمن اعتباراً لحالة الاجتماع بحالة الانفراد وقد بينا الحكم في كل فرد ولا تكون يده على نفسه مانعة من استيلاء الكفار على ما معه لقيام الرق المانع للملك بالاستيلاء كغيره وفي القاموس : المتاع المنفعة والسلعة والأداة وما تمتعت به من الحوائج .اهـ. والمراد الثاني هنا
The Book of Siyar, Chapter on Domination of Disbelievers. Taking the slave for free and other than it with the price. And if a musta'min buys a believing slave and enters him into their abode, or a slave becomes secure there then comes to us or we dominate over them, he is free. (Fath al-Qadir and Sharh al-Wiqaya): There is disagreement regarding when they take him forcefully and tie him. As for when it is not forceful, they do not own him by consensus. He becomes free. He said: And if he flees with a horse or goods, and a man buys it all from them, he takes the slave for free and other than it with the price, meaning according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: He takes the slave and what is with him with the price, considering the state of coming together as the state of being separate. And we have explained the ruling for each individual. And his hand over himself does not prevent the disbelievers' domination over what is with him due to the existence of slavery which prevents ownership by domination, like other than him. And in al-Qamus: al-mata' is benefit, commodities, tools, and what you benefit from of necessities. The second meaning is intended here. And the second meaning here is...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the chapter on the domination of disbelievers (istila' al-kuffar). The matn states a comprehensive ruling: if the kuffar take a slave and other property, the slave is taken without compensation (majjanan) but other goods must be paid for. Additionally, if a musta'min (a non-Muslim granted aman) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, or if a slave of a harbi converts to Islam and then comes to us or we conquer them, the slave becomes free. The commentary then clarifies that there is disagreement on the first part: if the kuffar take the slave forcefully and tie him, there is ikhtilaf; but if they take him without force, they do not own him by consensus. The phrase 'he becomes free' appears abruptly—likely a remnant. The commentary then discusses a scenario where a slave flees with a horse or goods, and a man buys the entire bundle from the kuffar. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer takes the slave for free (since the kuffar's ownership of the slave is defective) but must pay for the other goods. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the buyer pays for both the slave and the goods, considering the whole as one transaction. Their reasoning is that the state of being together (ijtima') should be treated like the state of being separate (infirad)—meaning each item should be ruled independently, but since they were taken together, the slave is not special. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the slave's own control over himself does not prevent the kuffar's domination over what he has, because slavery (riqq) prevents ownership by mere domination (istila'). The commentary then defines 'mata' from al-Qamus as benefit, commodities, tools, etc., and specifies that here it refers to the second meaning (commodities/goods). The phrase 'and the second meaning here' transitions to the next ruling.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim (harbi) who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary aman (safe conduct).
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims, not under any covenant.
استيلاء (istila'): Domination or seizure by force, especially by kuffar over Muslim property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, if a person buys a bundle containing a slave and a horse from harbi kuffar who had captured them, what must he pay?
A) Both the slave and the horse.
B) Only the slave.
C) Only the horse.
D) Nothing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a Muslim slave captured by harbi kuffar is not owned by them; thus when redeemed along with goods, the slave is taken without payment, while the goods are paid for.

12 min
Rulings on Musta'min Buying a Muslim Slave and Slave's Islam in Dar al-Harb
Explains two rulings with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for two scenarios involving slaves between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb, and explain the proofs and intra-Hanafi disagreements.

Core Idea
The obligation to free a Muslim from kufr overrides normal property rights in dar al-harb.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two issues that arise when Muslims and harbis interact across the dar al-Islam and dar al-harb boundary. The Hanafi school, led by Imam Abu Hanifa, prioritizes the freeing of Muslim slaves from non-Muslim control, using the concept of tabayun al-darayn as a substitute for express manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
Recognize that the passage discusses two distinct rulings: (a) a musta'min (harbi with aman) buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, and (b) a harbi's slave converts to Islam in dar al-harb.
Each has its own conditions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for the first issue
According to Abu Hanifa, the Muslim slave becomes free immediately upon entering dar al-harb. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that the slave remains in bondage because the removal of ownership was due by sale only.
The difference hinges on whether tabayun al-darayn can substitute for express manumission.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that freeing a Muslim from kufr is wajib. He draws a qiyas with a woman who converts to Islam in dar al-harb: the passage of three menstrual periods (iddah) substitutes for a formal judge's separation. Here, the difference of abodes substitutes for manumission.
The proof is based on the principle of necessity and the obligation to rescue Muslims.
4
State the second issue and its rulings
If a harbi's slave converts to Islam in dar al-harb and then escapes to dar al-Islam or the Muslims conquer the territory, he becomes free. If he converts but remains in dar al-harb without escape or conquest, he remains a slave until a Muslim or dhimmi buys him, at which point he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa.
The hadith of the slaves of Ta'if is cited as a precedent for freedom upon escape.
5
Note the conditions and exceptions
The first ruling only applies if the musta'min owned the slave in dar al-Islam; if the slave was captured from dar al-Islam, he does not become free. For the second ruling, if the master offers the slave for sale, the slave becomes free even before acceptance.
These conditions reflect the balance between freeing Muslims and respecting property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن ابتاع مستأمن عبداً مؤمناً وأدخله دارهم أو أمن عبد ثمة فجاءنا أو ظهرنا عليهم عتق بيان لمسألتين: الأولى : أن الحربي إذا دخل دارنا بأمان واشترى عبداً مسلماً وأدخله دار الحرب عتق عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا لأن الإزالة كانت مستحقة بطريق معين وهو البيع وقد انقطعت ولاية الجبر عليه فبقي في يده عبداً. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله أن تخليص المسلم عن ذل الكافر واجب فيقام الشرط وهو تباين الدارين مقام العلة وهو الإعتاق تخليصاً له كما يقام مضي ثلاث حيض مقام التفريق فيما إذا أسلمت المرأة في دار الحرب. قيد بكون الحربي ملكه في دار الإسلام لأن العبد المسلم إذا أسره الحربي من دار الإسلام وأدخله داره لا يعتق عليه اتفاقاً، أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عنده فللمانع من عمل المقتضي عمله وهو حق استرداد المسلم، وعلى الخلاف السابق لو أسلم عبد الحربي ولم يهرب إلى دار الإسلام حتى اشتراه مسلم أو ذمي أو حربي في دار الحرب يعتق عنده خلافاً لهما لأن العتق في دار الحرب يعتمد زوال القهر الخاص وقد عدم إذ زال قهره إلى المشتري فصار كما لو كان في يده، وله أن قهره زال حقيقة بالبيع وكان إسلامه يوجب إزالة قهره عنه إلا أنه تعذر الخطاب بالإزالة فأقيم ما له أثر في زوال الملك مقام الإزالة وهو البيع والتقييد بأيمان العبد اتفاقي إذ لو كان ذمياً فالحكم كذلك لأنه يجبر على بيعه ولا يمكن من إدخاله دار الحرب كما في النهاية الثانية : لو أسلم عبد الحربي ثم خرج إلينا أو ظهر على الدار فهو حر، وكذا إذا خرج عبيدهم إلى عسكر المسلمين فهم أحرار أن عبيداً من عبيد الطائف أسلموا وخرجوا إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقضى بعتقهم وقال : هم عتقاء الله تعالى. وقيد بخروجه أو ظهورنا لأنه إذا أسلم ولم يوجدا فهو رقيق إلى أن يشتريه مسلم أو ذمي فيعتق وفي شرح الطحاوي: إذا لم يوجدا لم يعتق إلا إذا عرض رضه المولى على البيع من مسلم أو كافر فحينئذ يعتق العبد قبل المشتري البيع أو لم يقبل، لأنه لما عرضه فقد رضي بزوال ملكه والتقييد بإيمانه في دار الحرب اتفاقي إذ لو خرج مراغماً لمولاه فأمن في دار الإسلام فالحكم كذلك بخلاف ما إذا خرج بإذن مولاه أو بأمره لحاجته فأسلم في
روي
He said: (And if a musta'min buys a believing slave and enters him into their abode, or a slave becomes secure there then comes to us or we dominate over them, he is free) Explanation of two issues: First: that if a harbi enters our abode with aman and buys a Muslim slave and enters him into dar al-harb, he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Sahibayn) said: No, because the removal (of bondage) was due by a specific path, which is sale, and the authority of compulsion is cut off, so he remains a slave in his hand. For Abu Hanifa: freeing the Muslim from the humiliation of the disbeliever is obligatory, so the condition (difference of abodes) stands in place of the cause (manumission) to free him, just as the passage of three menstrual periods stands in place of separation when a woman accepts Islam in dar al-harb. It is conditioned that the harbi owned him in dar al-Islam, because if a Muslim slave is captured by a harbi from dar al-Islam and entered into his abode, he does not become free by agreement—as for them (Sahibayn) it is apparent; as for him (Abu Hanifa), there is a preventer of the effect of the effective cause, which is the right of the Muslim to reclaim him. And on the previous disagreement, if a slave of a harbi accepts Islam and did not flee to dar al-Islam until a Muslim, dhimmi, or harbi buys him in dar al-harb, he becomes free according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them, because manumission in dar al-harb depends on the removal of specific compulsion, and it is absent since his compulsion has transferred to the buyer, so it is as if he was in his hand. For him, his compulsion was removed by the sale, and his Islam required removal of compulsion, but address to remove was not possible, so what affects ownership (sale) is placed in the position of removal. The restriction to the slave's faith is by agreement; if he was a dhimmi, the ruling is similar because he is forced to sell and cannot be entered into dar al-harb. As in al-Nihaya: Second issue: if a harbi slave accepts Islam and then comes out to us or we dominate the abode, he is free. Likewise, if their slaves come out to the Muslim army, they are free. Verily, slaves from the slaves of Ta'if accepted Islam and came out to the Messenger of Allah, and he ruled their freedom and said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' It is conditioned that he comes out or we dominate because if he accepts Islam and they are not found, he remains a slave until a Muslim or dhimmi buys him, then he becomes free. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If they are not found, he does not become free unless the master offers him for sale to a Muslim or disbeliever; then the slave becomes free whether the buyer accepts or not, because by offering he has consented to the removal of his ownership. The restriction to his faith in dar al-harb is by agreement; if he came out migrating from his master and was given aman in dar al-Islam, the ruling is similar, contrary to if he came out with his master's permission or by his order for his need, then he accepts Islam in...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn from the previous lesson. It clarifies two distinct issues embedded in the matn. The first issue: when a harbi enters dar al-Islam with aman (becoming a musta'min) and buys a Muslim slave, then takes him to dar al-harb. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave becomes free immediately upon entering dar al-harb. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that the slave's bondage was to be removed only through sale (the specific means), and since the musta'min's authority over him is not complete, he remains a slave. Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the obligation to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of kufr. He uses an analogy: just as the passage of three menstrual periods (iddah) serves as a substitute for the legal separation (tafriq) when a woman converts to Islam in dar al-harb while her husband remains a disbeliever, here the difference of abodes (tabayun al-darayn) serves as a substitute for the act of manumission to free the slave. The commentary adds a condition: this ruling only applies if the harbi owned the slave in dar al-Islam. If the slave was captured from dar al-Islam and taken to dar al-harb, all agree he does not become free because the Muslim community has the right to reclaim him (istirdad). The second issue: if a slave of a harbi converts to Islam in dar al-harb and then escapes to dar al-Islam, or if the Muslims conquer the territory, he becomes free. The commentary cites the precedent of the slaves of Ta'if who converted and came to the Prophet, and he declared them free. However, if the slave converts but remains in dar al-harb without coming out and without conquest, he remains a slave until a Muslim or dhimmi buys him, upon which he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. The commentary from Sharh al-Tahawi adds nuance: if the master offers the slave for sale to anyone, the slave becomes free even before acceptance, because the master's offer indicates consent to the removal of his ownership. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes that these rulings are motivated by the principle of releasing Muslims from the control of disbelievers.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who enters dar al-Islam with a safe conduct (aman).
تباين الدارين (tabayun al-darayn): The difference or separation between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb, a legal concept affecting status.
عتق حكمي (itq hukmi): A legal (constructive) manumission, not an express declaration, but resulting from a change in status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when a musta'min buys a Muslim slave in dar al-Islam and takes him to dar al-harb?
A) The slave remains a slave.
B) The slave becomes free.
C) The slave is returned to the seller.
D) The musta'min must free him immediately.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim slave in the hands of a harbi becomes free upon entering dar al-Islam or upon the Muslims' conquest, based on the obligation to free Muslims from kufr.

The Musta'min's Prohibited Ownership: Taking from Harbis under Aman
Ruling on musta'min taking property from harbis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a musta'min who takes property from harbis and explain why the property must be given in charity.

Core Idea
Taking from harbis under aman is treachery, prohibited, but ownership still arises—then must give charity.

When a Muslim merchant enters enemy territory with a guarantee of safety (aman), he is bound to uphold that trust. Taking any property from the harbis would be an act of treachery (ghadr), which is strictly forbidden. However, if he does take something, Islamic law does not nullify the acquisition; instead, it recognizes a form of ownership that is sinful to retain—called milk mahdhar—and requires the taker to purify his sin by giving the property in charity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject and context
The ruling concerns a Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb under aman (musta'min). Distinguish him from a captive or fighter. The key is the promise of safety he made.
The text uses 'tajiruna' (our merchant) to highlight the typical musta'min.
2
Determine the ruling on taking property
The act of taking property from harbis is haram because it constitutes ghadr (treachery). The musta'min guaranteed not to harm them, so any taking breaks that promise.
Exception: if the enemy king himself breaks the covenant first, the musta'min may retaliate.
3
Understand the legal consequence: milk mahdhar
Even though the act is haram, the taking still establishes ownership through physical possession (istila'). However, this ownership is 'prohibited ownership' (milk mahdhar), meaning the item is legitimately owned but its retention is sinful.
Analogous to other cases in Hanafi fiqh where a prohibited cause yields ownership.
4
Apply the required action: tasadduq (charity)
To purify the sin, the musta'min must give the acquired property in charity. He cannot keep it or use it for personal benefit. This applies to both goods and slave-girls.
For a slave-girl taken, he cannot have intercourse with her, nor can any subsequent buyer.
5
Distinguish from other scenarios
The ruling differs for captives (who may take property and kill), for the musta'min's own family members (who are not owned by harbis), and for goods usurped by the enemy first. The text also covers cases where the musta'min is faced with captured Muslims.
These distinctions are important for fatwa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن
دخل تاجرنا حرم تعرضه لشيء منهم فلو أخرج شيئاً ملكه ملكاً محظوراً فيتصدق به
أخره عن الاستيلاء لأن الاستيلاء يكون بالقهر والاستئمان يكون بعد القهر. قوله: (دخل تاجرنا) أي دخل المسلم دار الحرب بأمان، وعبر عنه بالتاجر لأنه لا يدخل دارهم إلا بأمان حفظاً لما له. وإنما حرم عليه لأنه ضمن بالاستئمان أن لا يتعرض لهم فالتعرض بعد ذلك يكون غدراً والغدر حرام إلا إذا غدر به ملكهم فأخذ ماله أو حبسه أو فعل غيره بعلم الملك ولم يمنعه لأنهم هم الذين نقضوا العهد. قيد بالتاجر لأن الأسير يباح له التعرض وإن أطلقوه طوعاً لأنه غير مستأمن فهو كالمتلصص فيجوز له أخذ المال وقتل النفس دون استباحة الفرج لأنه لا يحل إلا بالملك ولا ملك قبل الإحراز بدارنا إلا إذا وجد من لم يملكه أهل الحرب من امرأته وأم ولده ومدبرته فيباح له وطؤهن إلا إذا وطئهن أهل الحرب فتجب العدة للشبهة فلا يجوز وطؤهن حتى تنقضي عدتهن بخلاف أمته المأسورة لا يحل وطؤها مطلقاً لأنها مملوكة لهم. وأطلق الشيء فشمل النفوس والأموال حتى أمة التاجر المأسورة لأنها من أملاكهم، ولا يدخل تحته زوجته وأم ولده ومدبرته لأنهن غير مملوكات لهم فيجوز للتاجر التعرض لهن. وكذا لو أغار أهل الحرب الذين فيهم مسلمون مستأمنون على طائفة من المسلمين فأسروا ذراريهم فمروا بهم على أولئك المستأمنين وجب عليهم أن ينقضوا عهودهم ويقاتلوهم إذا كانوا يقدرون عليه لأنهم لا يملكون رقابهم فتقريرهم في أيديهم تقرير على الظلم، ولم يضمنوا ذلك لهم بخلاف الأموال لأنهم ملكوها بالإحراز وقد ضمنوا لهم أن لا يتعرضوا لأموالهم. وكذا لو كان المأخوذ ذراري الخوارج لأنهم مسلمون. ومن الفروع النفيسة ما في المبسوط: لو أغار قوم من أهل الحرب على أهل الدار التي فيهم المسلم المستأمن لا يحل له قتال هؤلاء الكفار إلا إن خاف على نفسه لأن القتال لما كان تعريضاً لنفسه على الهلاك لا يحل إلا لذلك أو لإعلاء كلمة الله وهو إذا لم يخف على نفسه ليس قتال هؤلاء إلا إعلاء كلمة الكفر. اهـ. وفي المحيط: مسلم دخل دار الحرب بأمان فجاء رجل من أهل الحرب بأمه أو بأم ولده أو بعمته أو بخالته قد قهرها ببيعها من المسلم المستأمن لا يشتريها منه لأن الحربي إن ملكها بالقهر فقد صارت حرة فإذا باعها فقد باع الحرة، ولو قهر حربي بعض أحرارهم ثم جاء بهم إلى المسلم المستأمن فباعهم منه ينظر؛ إن كان الحكم عندهم أن من قهر منهم صاحبه فقد صار ملكه جاز الشراء لأنه باع المملوك، وإن لم يملكه لا يجوز لأنه باع الحر.
قوله: (فلو أخرج شيئاً ملكه ملكاً محظوراً فيتصدق به) لورود الاستيلاء على مال مباح إلا أنه حصل بسبب الغدر فأوجب ذلك خبثاً فيه فيؤمر بالتصدق به. وهذا لأن الحظر فيه لا يمنع انعقاد السبب على ما بيناه. أفاد بالحظر مع وجوب التصدق أنه لو كان المأخوذ غدراً جارية لا يحل له وطؤها ولا للمشتري منه بخلاف المشتراة شراء فاسداً فإن حرمة وطئها على المشتري خاصة وتحل للمشتري منه لأن المنع منه لثبوت حق البائع في حق الاسترداد وببيع المشتري انقطع حقه ذلك لأنه باع بيعاً صحيحاً فلم يثبت له حق الاسترداد وهناك الكراهة للغدر، والمشتري الثاني كالأول فيه. وفي الولوالجية: مسلم تزوج امرأة في دار الحرب وكانت كافرة فأعطى للأب صداقها فأضمر في قلبه أنه يبيعها فخرج بها إلى دار الإسلام فأراد بيعها فالبيع باطل وهي حرة يريد به إذا خرجت معه طوعاً لأن أهل الحرب إنما يملكون بالقهر في دار الحرب فإذا لم يقهر في دار الحرب وخرجت معه إلى دار الإسلام بغير قهر لا تصير ملكاً له. اهـ. وفي فتح القدير: واعلم أنهم أخذوا في تصويرها ما إذا أضمر في نفسه أنه يخرجها ليبيعها ولا بد منه لأنه لو أخرجها كرهاً لا لهذا الغرض بل لاعتقاده أن له أن يذهب بزوجته حيث شاء إذا أوفاها معجل مهرها ينبغي أن لا يملكها. اهـ. وقيد بالإخراج لأنها إذا غصب شيئاً في دار الحرب وجب عليه التوبة وهي لا تحصل إلا بالرد عليهم فأشبه المشتري شراء فاسداً؛ كذا في المحيط.
Chapter of the Musta'min. Our merchant entered; it is prohibited to harm any of them. So if he takes something out, he owns it with a prohibited ownership, so he gives it in charity. [Commentary:] He placed it after the chapter on domination because domination is by force and trust (aman) is after force. His saying: (Our merchant entered) means a Muslim entered the abode of war with aman, and he was called a merchant because he only enters their abode with aman to protect his property. It is prohibited for him because he guaranteed by the aman not to harm them, so harming after that is treachery, and treachery is prohibited unless their king betrays him by taking his property or imprisoning him or doing something else with the king's knowledge and he did not prevent him, because they are the ones who broke the covenant. He restricted it to the merchant because the captive is permitted to harm them even if they release him voluntarily because he is not a musta'min; he is like a looter, so it is permissible for him to take property and kill life, but not to engage in sexual relations because that is only permissible through ownership, and there is no ownership before transport to our abode, unless he finds a woman whom the people of war do not own, such as his wife, his umm walad, or his mudabbara, then he may have intercourse with them, unless the people of war have had intercourse with them, then idda is necessary due to ambiguity, so he may not have intercourse until their idda expires, unlike his captured slave-girl, it is absolutely not permissible to have intercourse with her because she is owned by them. The word 'thing' is general and includes lives and property, even the captured slave-girl of the merchant because she is among their possessions, but it does not include his wife, umm walad, or mudabbara because they are not owned by them, so the merchant may harm them. Likewise, if the people of war who have Muslim musta'mins among them attack a group of Muslims and capture their children, and then pass by those musta'mins, it is obligatory for them to break their covenants and fight them if they are able to, because they do not own their persons, so keeping them in their hands is oppression. They are not liable for that, unlike property because they own it by transport and have guaranteed not to harm their property. Likewise if the captured are the children of the Khawarij because they are Muslims. Among the subtle branches in al-Mabsut: If a group from the people of war attack the people of a village in which there is a Muslim musta'min, it is not permissible for him to fight these disbelievers unless he fears for himself, because fighting is endangering his life, which is not permissible except for that or for elevating the word of God, and when he does not fear for himself, fighting these is only elevating the word of disbelief. End. In al-Muhit: A Muslim entered dar al-harb with aman, then a man from the people of war brought his mother or his umm walad or his paternal aunt or his maternal aunt whom he had conquered by selling her to the Muslim musta'min; he should not buy her from him, because if the warrier owned her by force, she became free, so if he sells her, he has sold a free person. And if a warrier conquers some of their free men and then brings them to the Muslim musta'min and sells them to him, it is considered: if the ruling among them is that whoever conquers his companion becomes his owner, then purchase is permissible because he sold an owned person; if he does not own, it is not permissible because he sold a free person. His saying: (So if he takes something out, he owns it with a prohibited ownership, so he gives it in charity) because taking possession occurs on permissible property, but it happened due to treachery, so that caused impurity in it, so he is ordered to give it in charity. This is because the prohibition does not prevent the cause from taking effect as we explained. He indicated by the prohibition along with the obligation of charity that if the taken item by treachery is a slave-girl, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her nor for the one who buys from him, unlike the one bought with a corrupt purchase, as the prohibition of having intercourse with her is specific to the buyer and is permissible for the one from whom he bought because the prohibition is due to the seller's right of repossession, and by the buyer's sale, that right is cut off because he sold a valid sale, so no right of repossession is established, and there the dislike is due to treachery, and the second buyer is like the first. In al-Walwalijiyya: A Muslim married a woman in dar al-harb and she was a disbeliever, he gave her father her dowry, and he harbored in his heart that he would sell her, then he took her out to dar al-Islam and wanted to sell her. The sale is void and she is free, meaning if she came out with him willingly, because the people of war only own by force in dar al-harb, and if he did not use force in dar al-harb and she came out with him to dar al-Islam without force, she does not become his property. End. In Fath al-Qadir: Know that they took in depicting it that he harbored in his heart that he would take her out to sell her, and it is necessary, because if he took her out unwillingly not for this purpose but because he believed that he could take his wife wherever he wished when he paid her immediate dower, then he ought not to own her. End. He restricted it to ikhraj (taking out) because if he usurped something in dar al-harb, repentance is obligatory upon him and that is only achieved by returning it to them, so it is similar to one who buys with a corrupt purchase. So in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the ruling for a Muslim musta'min (one granted aman) entering dar al-harb. The chapter begins by stating that a Muslim merchant who enters enemy territory with aman is prohibited from harming any of the enemy's persons or property. This is because he has guaranteed safety in return for aman; any harm would be treachery (ghadr), which is haram. However, if the enemy ruler himself breaks the covenant by harming the musta'min, then the musta'min may retaliate. The ruling distinguishes between a musta'min merchant and a captive: a captive, even if released, is not a musta'min and may take property and even kill, but not engage in sexual relations with enemy women. The musta'min is also allowed to have intercourse with his own wife, umm walad, and mudabbara who are with him, as they are not owned by the enemy. But if they have been molested by the enemy, he must wait for their idda. If a musta'min takes something from the enemy, he legally owns it due to possession (istila'), but because it was obtained through treachery, the ownership is 'prohibited ownership' (milk mahdhar), and he must give it in charity. This rule extends to a slave-girl taken; he cannot have intercourse with her, and neither can a purchaser, unlike a corrupt sale where the prohibition is only on the buyer. The passage also discusses cases where the musta'min is confronted with enemy captives or free persons being sold to him, and shares the details that depend on the enemy's legal system. Overall, the core principle is that the musta'min must uphold his aman and cannot benefit from treachery.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person who enters enemy territory under a guarantee of safety (aman).
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; breaking a promise of safety.
ملك محظور (milk mahdhar): Ownership that is established by law but is sinful to retain; requires disposal by charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what must a musta'min do with property he takes from a harbi in Dar al-Harb?
A) Return it to the harbi.
B) Keep it as lawful spoils.
C) Give it in charity.
D) Destroy it.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min who takes property from harbis owns it but must give it in charity to expiate the sin of treachery.

Jurisdiction Over Past Actions: Musta'min and Harbi in Dar al-Islam
Explaining why the judge does not adjudicate loans/usurpation between musta'min and harbi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge does not adjudicate loans or usurpation between a musta'min and a harbi for actions in dar al-harb and explain the two reasons: lack of past jurisdiction for loans and inability to enforce for usurpation.

Core Idea
Islamic judges only adjudicate actions that occurred under Islamic jurisdiction; past actions in dar al-harb are not subject to Islamic courts.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key rule in Hanafi siyar: when a musta'min (a non-Muslim granted safe passage) and a harbi (an enemy non-Muslim) have a financial dispute from dar al-harb and then both enter dar al-Islam, the Islamic judge does not hear the case. The reasoning hinges on the nature of jurisdiction and enforceability. We will unpack the ruling and the subtle linguistic issue about whether 'i'dana' includes loans or only sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties and Transactions
The case involves two non-Muslims: a musta'min (under safe passage) and a harbi (enemy). The transactions are a loan (i'dana) or usurpation (ghasb) that occurred while they were both in dar al-harb.
The musta'min has pledged to follow Islamic law only from entry into dar al-Islam.
2
State the Ruling
When both come to dar al-Islam, the judge 'does not adjudicate anything' (lam yaqdi bi shay'). The ruling is absolute: no judgment is passed on either claim.
3
Analyze the Reason for Loans
Judgment requires jurisdiction (wilaya) over the parties at the time of the transaction. At the time of the loan, the musta'min was not under Islamic jurisdiction for past acts—he only committed to it for the future. The harbi never submitted to Islamic law. Hence, no jurisdiction exists now for a past act.
Jurisdiction is retrospective only if the party was already bound by Islamic law at the time of action.
4
Analyze the Reason for Usurpation
Judgment also requires the ability to enforce the ruling (tamakkun min al-ilzam). In usurpation, the musta'min does not accept the judgment for past acts, and the harbi is not subject to Islamic law. Therefore, enforcement is impossible.
The musta'min's future commitment does not oblige him to submit to a judgment for past acts.
5
Examine the Linguistic Nuance: Does 'Adana' Include Loans?
The word 'adana' (used in the matn) literally means 'gave a loan' or 'incurred a debt'. Some copies restrict it to sales (i.e., credit sales), but the preferred view (as in al-Bahr al-Raiq) includes qard (interest-free loans). The commentary cites lexicons to support the broader meaning.
This distinction affects the scope of the ruling: if 'adana' only means sale, then loans (qard) might be treated differently. But the stronger view includes loans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فإن أدانه حربي أو أدان حربياً أو غصب أحدهما صاحبه وخرجا إلينا لم يقض بشيء) أما الإدانة فلأن القضاء يعتمد الولاية ولا ولاية وقت الإدانة أصلاً ولا وقت القضاء على المستأمن لأنه ما التزم حكم الإسلام فيما مضى من أفعاله وإنما التزم ذلك في المستقبل. وأما الغصب فلأن القضاء يعتمد التمكن من الإلزام والتمكن من الإلزام لا يحصل لأن أحدهما مستأمن والآخر حربي فالمستأمن لا يلزم نفسه بالحكم ولا يلزم الحربي لأنه ليس من أهل دار الإسلام. وظاهره تخصيصه بالبيع وأنه لا يشمل القرض كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها وظاهره عدم تخصيصه الخ وهذا هو المناسب. قال في النهر بعد ذكره ما في القاموس: لكن في المغرب أدنته ودينته أقرضته. وعلى هذا في الكتاب يشمل القرض أيضاً لكن في طلبة الطلبة أدان بالتشديد من باب الافتعال أي قبل الدين والدين غير القرض لأن القرض اسم لما يقرض ويقبض والدين اسم لما يصير في الذمة وقد قيل إن اسم الدين شامل لجميع ما يجب في الذمة بالعقد والاستهلاك أو بالاستقراض؛ كذا في
His saying: (If a harbi gives a loan to him, or he gives a loan to a harbi, or one of them usurps from the other, and they come out to us, nothing is adjudged.) As for the loan, because judgment depends on jurisdiction, and there was no jurisdiction at the time of the loan at all, nor at the time of judgment on the musta'min, because he did not commit to the rule of Islam regarding his past actions, but only committed to it for the future. As for usurpation, because judgment depends on the ability to enforce, and that is not attained because one is a musta'min and the other a harbi; the musta'min does not bind himself to the judgment, nor does the harbi bind because he is not from the people of dar al-Islam. Its apparent meaning is that it is specific to sale and does not include loan; so in some copies, and in some its apparent meaning is that it is not specific, etc., and this is appropriate. He said in al-Nahr after mentioning what is in al-Qamus: But in al-Maghrib 'adantu' and 'dintu' both mean 'I gave a loan'. Based on this, in the book it includes loan as well. But in Talbat al-Talaba, 'adana' with tashdid is from bab al-ifti'al meaning he accepted the debt, and debt is not a loan because loan is what is lent and received and debt is what becomes in the liability. And it has been said that the name debt includes all that is obligatory in the liability by contract, consumption, or borrowing. So in ...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if a Muslim musta'min and a harbi had a financial transaction (loan or usurpation) while in dar al-harb, and then both come to dar al-Islam, an Islamic judge does not adjudicate the case. The reason for loans is that the judge's authority depends on his jurisdiction over the parties at the time of the contract. At the time of the loan, the musta'min was under the aman but not under Islamic jurisdiction for past actions; he only commits to follow Islamic law from the time he enters dar al-Islam. For usurpation, the judge cannot enforce the ruling because the musta'min does not accept the judgment against him for past acts, and the harbi is not subject to Islamic law. The text also discusses a linguistic issue: whether the word 'adana' (lent) in the matn is specific to sale or includes loans (qard). Some copies say it is specific, others say it is general. The preferred view is that it includes loans, as supported by al-Nahr and al-Maghrib. However, Talbat al-Talaba distinguishes between 'adana' (accepting a debt) and 'dana' (giving a loan). The commentary leaves the matter open, noting that debt (dayn) can include what is owed by contract, consumption, or borrowing.

Key Terms
إدانة (i'dana): Giving a loan; in the context, it is used for both lending and borrowing transactions.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation of property; taking something by force without right.
ولاية (wilaya): Jurisdiction or authority of the judge to adjudicate a case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the judge not adjudicate a loan between a musta'min and a harbi that took place in dar al-harb?
A) Because the transaction is invalid in Islamic law
B) Because the musta'min did not commit to Islamic law for past actions
C) Because the harbi is not present in court
D) Because the loan requires a written contract

Key Takeaway
A judge's jurisdiction over financial disputes depends on the parties' submission to Islamic law at the time of the action, not at the time of adjudication.

Judgment on Harbi Transactions After Entering Dar al-Islam
Commentary on matn regarding judgment for musta'min harbis on debt and usurpation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on debt and usurpation between two warring people who become musta'min or embrace Islam, and explain the rationale based on jurisdiction and ownership.

Core Idea
Islamic courts do not reverse transactions validly concluded in dar al-harb; debt is enforced upon conversion but usurpation is not.

When two people from enemy territory (harbis) engage in transactions like credit sales or usurpation and then enter Dar al-Islam with a safe conduct or after embracing Islam, the Hanafi school distinguishes between enforcement of debt and usurpation. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this distinction and how the court's jurisdiction applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The passage presents two cases: two harbis who engage in debt (idana) or usurpation (ghasb) and then either enter with aman or embrace Islam and exit dar al-harb. First, determine the parties and the time of the transaction.
The transaction occurs while both are still harbis in dar al-harb.
2
Apply the Ruling for Musta'min
If they enter with aman, no judgment (qada) is given on any prior transaction. This is because the court's jurisdiction does not extend to acts done while they were not subject to Islamic law.
3
Apply the Ruling for Muslims
If they embrace Islam and then exit dar al-harb, the court will judge (qada) the debt but not the usurpation. The debt is valid due to mutual consent, and Islam establishes the authority of the court. Usurpation, however, resulted in ownership in dar al-harb and is not reversed.
Key distinction: debt is an obligation, usurpation transfers ownership.
4
Consider the Muslim Who Usurps in Dar al-Harb
The text notes that if a Muslim enters dar al-harb with aman and usurps something, he is given a fatwa to return it but is not compelled by the court. This contrasts with the harbi case.
Fatwa vs. qada: ruling for conscience vs. enforceable judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأدانه حربي قوله: (وكذلك لو كانا حربيين وفعلا ذلك ثم استأمنا) أي الإدانة والغصب ثم دخلا دارنا بأمان لم يقض بشيء لما بيناه وفي المحيط: خرج حربي مع مسلم إلى العسكر وادعى المسلم أنه أسير وقال كنت مستأمناً فالقول للحربي إلا إذا قامت قرينة ككونه مكتوفاً أو مغلولاً أو كان عدد من المسلمين قوله: (وإن خرجا مسلمین قضى بالدين بينهما لا بالغصب) أي أسلم الحربيان في دار الحرب ثم خرجا مسلمين بعد الإدانة أو الغصب لأن المداينة وقعت صحيحة لوقوعها بالتراضي والولاية ثابتة حالة القضاء لالتزامهما الأحكام بالإسلام وأما الغصب فلما بيناه أنه ملكه ولا خبث في ملك الحربي حتى يؤمر بالرد وقد قدمنا أن المسلم إذا دخل دارهم بأمان أو غصب منهم شيئاً يفتى بالرد وإن لم يقض عليه قيد بالقضاء لأن المسلم يفتى برد المغصوب وإن كان لا يحكم عليه به لأنه غدر كذا ذكره الشارح وسكت عن الإفتاء بقضاء الدين وفي فتح القدير: يفتى بأنه يجب عليه قضاء الدين فما بينه وبين الله تعالى وذكر الشارحون أن الإدانة البيع بالدين والاستدانة الابتياع بالدين والظاهر عدم تخصيصه بالبيع وأنه لا يشمل القرض لما في القاموس: أدان واستدان وتدين أخذ ديناً والدين ماله أجل ومالا أجل له فقرض وأدان اشترى بالدين أو باع بالدين ضد اهـ مع أنه في الحكم هنا لا فرق بينهما لأن أحدهما لو أقرض الآخر في دار الحزب شيئاً ثم خرجا لم يقض بشيء
A warring person lent [something]. His statement: (And similarly if they were two warring people and they did that then they sought aman) meaning the lending and usurpation, then they entered our land with aman, nothing is judged for anything due to what we explained. And in al-Muhit: A warring person went out with a Muslim to the army camp and the Muslim claimed he was a captive and said 'I was a musta'min', so the statement is of the warring person unless there is circumstantial evidence like being tied or shackled or there was a number of Muslims. His statement: (And if they exit as Muslims, judgment is given for the debt between them, not for the usurpation) meaning the two warring people embraced Islam in dar al-harb then exited as Muslims after the lending or usurpation because the lending occurred validly due to mutual consent, and authority is established at the time of judgment due to their commitment to Islamic rulings by Islam. As for usurpation, due to what we explained that it became his property and there is no impurity in the property of a warring person so that he would be ordered to return it. And we have already stated that if a Muslim enters their land with aman or usurps something from them, he is given a fatwa to return it although not judged against him. It is restricted to judgment because the Muslim is given a fatwa to return the usurped property even if not judged against him due to treachery; as the commentator mentioned and remained silent about giving fatwa regarding repayment of debt. And in Fath al-Qadir: He is given a fatwa that he must repay the debt, so between him and Allah Almighty. And the commentators mentioned that al-idana is selling on credit and al-istidana is buying on credit, and the apparent is that it is not restricted to sale and that it does not include loan because in al-Qamus: Adana, istadana, and tadayyana meaning took a debt, and al-dayn is property with a term and without a term, so qard, and adana means bought on credit or sold on credit opposite. While in the ruling here there is no difference between them because if one of them lent the other something in dar al-harb then they exited, nothing is judged.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq discussing the rulings when two warring people (harbi) have engaged in a transaction (like lending or usurpation) and then enter dar al-Islam with aman or after embracing Islam. The commentator explains that if they enter with aman, Islamic courts do not judge on those matters because at the time of the transaction they were not subject to Islamic law. However, if they embrace Islam and then exit dar al-harb as Muslims, the court will enforce the debt (because it was a valid mutual contract) but not the usurpation (because the usurped property became owned by the usurper in dar al-harb and there is no impurity in a harbi's ownership to necessitate return). The commentator also discusses a scenario from al-Muhit where a Muslim and a harbi dispute over status; the harbi's word is taken unless there is evidence. Additionally, there is a lexical discussion about the term 'idana' (lending) and whether it includes qard (loan); the author concludes that in this ruling, both are treated the same. The ruling that nothing is judged when they come as musta'min applies to both debt and usurpation.

Key Terms
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a safe conduct (aman).
الإدانة (al-idana): Selling on credit (or lending money on credit). The commentator discusses whether it includes qard (loan).
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation of property; taking property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two warring people (harbis) engage in a credit sale (idana) and then usurpation (ghasb) while in dar al-harb. They both embrace Islam and exit to Dar al-Islam. Which of the following is the Hanafi court's ruling?
A) The court judges both the debt and the usurpation.
B) The court judges the debt but not the usurpation.
C) The court judges the usurpation but not the debt.
D) The court judges neither.

Key Takeaway
Debt is enforced after conversion because it is a valid consensual obligation; usurpation is not reversed because ownership in dar al-harb is recognized.

The Meaning of Idana and the Fatwa vs. Judicial Ruling Distinction
Commentary on matn regarding judgment for musta'min harbis on debt and usurpation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain whether 'idana' includes qard (loan) and distinguish between a judicial ruling (qada) and a non-binding fatwa in the context of musta'min property.

Core Idea
Idana in this ruling includes both credit sales and loans, but the court's judgment differs from the mufti's advice: the court does not compel return of usurped property, yet the mufti may recommend it.

The commentators of Kanz al-Daqaiq delve into the precise meaning of 'idana' (credit transaction) and whether it covers loans (qard). Additionally, they highlight a crucial distinction between what a judge can enforce (qada) and what a mufti advises (fatwa). This lesson clarifies these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Lexical Definition
The commentators cite al-Qamus to define idana and its derivatives. Note that idana can mean selling on credit or buying on credit, and it is related to dayn (debt). The key question: does it include qard (loan)? The passage initially suggests idana does not include qard based on the lexical definition, but then states that in this ruling there is no difference between them.
2
Apply the Ruling to Both Idana and Qard
Even if idana is linguistically restricted to credit sales, the ruling that 'nothing is judged' or 'debt is judged' applies equally to qard because both are obligations of payment. The 'illah (effective cause) is the existence of a debt obligation, not the specific contract type.
3
Differentiate Between Qada and Fatwa
The text mentions that a Muslim who usurps from a harbi is given a fatwa to return the property but is not compelled by the court. Similarly, for a musta'min harbi, the court does not judge, but a fatwa may still exist. Understand that qada is an enforceable judgment by a qadi, while fatwa is a non-binding religious guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأدانه حربي قوله: (وكذلك لو كانا حربيين وفعلا ذلك ثم استأمنا) أي الإدانة والغصب ثم دخلا دارنا بأمان لم يقض بشيء لما بيناه وفي المحيط: خرج حربي مع مسلم إلى العسكر وادعى المسلم أنه أسير وقال كنت مستأمناً فالقول للحربي إلا إذا قامت قرينة ككونه مكتوفاً أو مغلولاً أو كان عدد من المسلمين قوله: (وإن خرجا مسلمین قضى بالدين بينهما لا بالغصب) أي أسلم الحربيان في دار الحرب ثم خرجا مسلمين بعد الإدانة أو الغصب لأن المداينة وقعت صحيحة لوقوعها بالتراضي والولاية ثابتة حالة القضاء لالتزامهما الأحكام بالإسلام وأما الغصب فلما بيناه أنه ملكه ولا خبث في ملك الحربي حتى يؤمر بالرد وقد قدمنا أن المسلم إذا دخل دارهم بأمان أو غصب منهم شيئاً يفتى بالرد وإن لم يقض عليه قيد بالقضاء لأن المسلم يفتى برد المغصوب وإن كان لا يحكم عليه به لأنه غدر كذا ذكره الشارح وسكت عن الإفتاء بقضاء الدين وفي فتح القدير: يفتى بأنه يجب عليه قضاء الدين فما بينه وبين الله تعالى وذكر الشارحون أن الإدانة البيع بالدين والاستدانة الابتياع بالدين والظاهر عدم تخصيصه بالبيع وأنه لا يشمل القرض لما في القاموس: أدان واستدان وتدين أخذ ديناً والدين ماله أجل ومالا أجل له فقرض وأدان اشترى بالدين أو باع بالدين ضد اهـ مع أنه في الحكم هنا لا فرق بينهما لأن أحدهما لو أقرض الآخر في دار الحزب شيئاً ثم خرجا لم يقض بشيء

Duration of Stay for Musta'min and Ruling on Entering without Aman
Fasl commentary on musta'min stay and unauthorised entry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum stay for a musta'min, identify when an unauthorised entrant becomes fay, and explain the Hanafi dispute on captor's right.

Core Idea
A musta'min cannot stay longer than one year without jizya; an unauthorised entrant is fay, but captor's exclusive right is disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses two interconnected issues of international law (siyar): the maximum duration a temporary warring visitor (musta'min) may reside in dar al-Islam, and the fate of a warring person who enters without any safe conduct. Understanding these rules is essential for grasping how the Hanafi school balances practical needs for trade with security concerns, and how intra-madhhab differences emerge over property rights.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
Identify the musta'min (one who enters with aman) from the unauthorised entrant (no aman). Their rulings differ fundamentally.
The first is a temporary safe visitor; the second is considered a potential belligerent.
2
State the musta'min's one-year limit
The musta'min cannot stay longer than a year. If the Imam tells him that staying a year requires jizya, he becomes a dhimmi if he stays. The reason is to prevent espionage while allowing trade.
The one-year period is tied to the obligation of jizya.
3
Explain the fay ruling for unauthorised entry
A warring person entering without aman becomes fay (spoils for the Muslim community). If he claims aman or messenger status, evidence is required.
Messengers with recognizable letters are safe.
4
Describe the intra-Hanafi dispute on captor's right
Abu Hanifa holds that such an entrant is general fay, not specific to the captor. The Sahibayn say the captor has exclusive right. Different implications for the Haram and conversion before capture.
Also a dispute on capturing in the Haram and on whether conversion before capture frees him.
5
Cover special cases
Return to dar al-harb nullifies fay status; a Muslim's claim of granting aman requires two witnesses. Finally, observing the rule that staying the Imam's specified period makes him a dhimmi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل لا يمكن مستأمن أن يقيم فينا سنة وقيل له إن أقمت سنة وضع عليك الجزية لأن الحربي لا يمكن من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا باسترقاق أو جزية لأنه يصير عيناً لهم وعوناً علينا فتلتحق المضرة بالمسلمين ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة لأن في منعها قطع الميرة والجلب وسد باب التجارة ففصلنا بينهما بسنة لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية فتكون الإقامة لمصلحة الجزية قيد بالمستأمن لأنه لو دخل دارنا بلا أمان فهو وما معه فيء فإن قال دخلت بأمان لم يصدق وأخذ ولو قال أنا رسول فإن وجد معه كتاب يعرف أنه كتاب ملكهم بعلامة تعرف ذلك كان آمناً فإن الرسول لا يحتاج إلى أمان خاص بل بكونه رسولاً يأمن وإن لم يعرف فهو زور فيكون هو وما معه فيئاً وإن دخل دار الإسلام بلا أمان فأخذه واحد من المسلمين لا يختص به عند أبي حنيفة بل يكون فيئاً لجماعة المسلمين وظاهر قولهما أنه يختص به ولو دخل الحرم قبل أن يؤخذ فعند أبي حنيفة يؤخذ ويكون فيئاً للمسلمين وعلى قولهما لا ولكن لا يطعَم ولا يسقى ولا يؤذى ولا يخرج كذا في فتح القدير وفي المحيط إذا دخل دارنا بلا أمان فهو فيء عند الإمام أخذ قبل الإسلام أو بعده وعندهما إن أسلم قبل الأخذ فهو حر ولو رجع هذا الحربي إلى دار الحرب خرج من أن يكون فيئاً وعاد حراً ولو قال رجل من المسلمين أنا أمنته لم يصدق إلا أن يشهد رجلان أنه أمنه قوله فإن مكث سنة فهو ذمي إن مكث المدة المضروبة فهو ذمي لأنه لما أقامها بعد تقدم الإمام إليه صار ملتزماً للجزية فيصير ذمياً فمراده من السنة ما وقته الإمام له سواء كانت سنة أو أقل كالشهر والشهرين وظاهر ما في الكتاب أن قول الإمام له ما ذكر شرط
Section: A musta'min cannot reside among us for a year, and it is said to him: If you stay for a year, jizya is imposed upon you. Because a warring person cannot be allowed permanent residence in our land except by enslavement or jizya, because he becomes an eye for them and a help against us, causing harm to the Muslims. He can be allowed a short stay because forbidding it would cut off food supplies and imports and close the door of trade. So we distinguished between them by a year because it is the period in which jizya becomes obligatory, so the residence is for the benefit of jizya. It is restricted to the musta'min because if he enters our land without aman, he and what he has are fay. If he says 'I entered with aman', he is not believed and is taken. If he says 'I am a messenger', and a book is found with him that is known to be the book of their king with a known sign, then he is safe, because the messenger does not need a special aman; rather, by being a messenger he is safe. If it is not known, it is false, so he and what he has are fay. If he enters dar al-Islam without aman and one of the Muslims captures him, it is not specific to him according to Abu Hanifa, rather it is fay for the entire Muslim community. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is specific to him. If he enters the Haram before being captured, then according to Abu Hanifa he is captured and becomes fay for the Muslims; according to them, no, but he is not fed, given drink, harmed, or expelled. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If he enters our land without aman, he is fay according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), whether he is captured before or after Islam. According to them, if he embraces Islam before capture, he is free. If this warring person returns to dar al-harb, he exits from being fay and becomes free. And if a Muslim man says 'I have granted him aman', he is not believed unless two men testify that he granted him aman. His statement: (If he stays a year, he is a dhimmi.) If he stays the specified period, he is a dhimmi, because when he stays after the Imam's advance to him, he becomes committed to jizya, so he becomes a dhimmi. His intent by 'year' is the period that the Imam specifies for him, whether it is a year or less like a month or two months. And the apparent from the book is that the Imam's statement to him what was mentioned is a condition.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This 'fasl' (section) from the sharh deals with two interconnected issues. First, the maximum duration a musta'min (a person from dar al-harb with temporary safe conduct) can stay in dar al-Islam: he cannot stay longer than a year, and if he intends to stay a year, he must be told that jizya will be imposed. The reason is that prolonged stay could lead to espionage or harm, but short stays for trade or supply are allowed. The one-year limit is chosen because it is the period for jizya obligation. Second, the ruling on a warring person who enters dar al-Islam without any aman: such a person and his property become fay (spoils) for the Muslims. If he claims he entered with aman, he is not believed unless he has evidence. If he claims to be a messenger and has a recognizable letter, he is safe. If a Muslim captures him, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: Abu Hanifa says he is fay for the entire Muslim community, not specific to the captor; the Sahibayn say the captor has exclusive right. If he enters the Haram (Mecca) before capture, Abu Hanifa says he can still be captured and made fay; the Sahibayn say he cannot be captured but is denied sustenance. If he converts to Islam before capture, according to the Sahibayn he becomes free. If he returns to dar al-harb, he is no longer fay. A Muslim's claim of granting aman is not accepted without two witnesses. Finally, if the musta'min stays the period set by the Imam (which may be less than a year), he becomes a dhimmi.

Key Terms
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A warring person who enters dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct (aman) for trade or similar.
الفيء (al-fay'): Spoils taken from warring people without battle, including property of those entering without aman.
الجزية (al-jizyah): A poll tax imposed on non-Muslims living under Muslim protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a warring person enters dar al-Islam without aman and claims to be a messenger but brings a letter not recognized, what happens?
A) He is given aman pending verification.
B) He and his property are considered fay.
C) He is allowed to stay for one year.
D) He is immediately enslaved.

Key Takeaway
The musta'min's stay in dar al-Islam is limited to one year to prevent harm, while an unauthorised entrant becomes fay, subject to an important intra-Hanafi dispute on property rights.

Musta'man and the One-Year Jizya Rule
Main ruling on musta'man stay and jizya obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a musta'man's stay duration and explain the istihsan-based dalil that underpins it.

Core Idea
A musta'man must leave or pay jizya after one year; short stays are permitted to preserve trade.

A musta'man (temporary safe-conduct holder) cannot settle indefinitely in Dar al-Islam. This lesson explores the Hanafi rule that if he stays a full year, jizya becomes obligatory. The key is balancing security concerns against the need for trade and supplies, with the one-year threshold being the period after which jizya is due. The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference) for public interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Condition
State the ruling: a musta'man cannot reside for a year without jizya. The condition is a full year's stay (or the period set by the imam).
The ruling is a prohibition on prolonged stay without financial contribution.
2
Understand the Legal Category
Recognise this as a wajib ruling on the imam (to impose jizya) and on the musta'man (to pay or leave). The authority is Zahir al-Riwayah.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih; some schools may not impose jizya after one year.
3
Analyse the Dalil: Istihsan
The proof is rational istihsan. Permanent harbi residence harms Muslims (spy, aid enemy) so it is prohibited. But short stays are allowed to avoid cutting trade. The distinction is at one year because jizya becomes due at that point.
The dalil is aqli (rational) based on maslaha and darura.
4
Trace the Ta'lil (Rationale)
The rationale: a musta'man becomes 'an eye and a helper' if he stays long. Short stays are beneficial for trade. The one-year mark aligns with the jizya obligation period.
The commentator uses the phrase 'فصلنا بينهما بسنة' to show the demarcation.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Imagine a merchant from a hostile territory visits with aman for a few weeks. That is allowed. If he stays 11 months and intends to stay indefinitely, he must leave before the year ends or pay jizya.
The imam can set a shorter period if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل لا يمكن مستأمن أن يقيم فينا سنة وقيل له إن أقمت سنة وضع عليك الجزية لأن الحربي لا يمكن من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا باسترقاق أو جزية لأنه يصير عيناً لهم وعوناً علينا فتلتحق المضرة بالمسلمين، ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة لأن في منعها قطع الميرة والجلب وسد باب التجارة ففصلنا بينهما بسنة لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية فتكون الإقامة لمصلحة الجزية.
A chapter: A musta'man cannot reside among us for a year, and it is said to him: 'If you stay a year, jizya will be imposed upon you.' Because a harbi cannot be allowed permanent residence in our abode except through enslavement or jizya, since he becomes an eye for them and a helper against us, causing harm to Muslims. But he may be allowed a short stay because preventing it would cut off supplies and trade. So we distinguish between the two by a year, because it is a period in which jizya becomes obligatory, thus the stay is for the benefit of jizya.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanded by the commentator. The passage states the default rule: a musta'man (a non-Muslim granted temporary safe conduct) may not reside indefinitely in Dar al-Islam. If he stays for a full year, he must either pay jizya or depart. The underlying reason (ta'lil) is that a long-term harbi presence poses a security risk—he could act as a spy or aid the enemy. However, short stays are permitted to avoid disrupting beneficial trade and supplies. The one-year threshold is chosen because it is the period after which jizya becomes payable, making the stay conditional on financial contribution. The commentator explains this using rational proof (maslaha and darura).

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'man): A non-Muslim who enters Dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct (aman) for a specific purpose.
جزية (jizya): Annual poll tax imposed on non-Muslims in exchange for protection and residence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a musta'man allowed a short stay but not a full year?
A) Because a short stay does not cause harm
B) Because a year is the minimum for jizya liability
C) To avoid cutting off trade and supplies
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
A musta'man cannot reside beyond one year without jizya because of security concerns, but short stays are allowed to preserve trade.

8 min
Fay' vs. Individual Ownership: The Captured Harbi Without Aman
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ownership when a harbi is captured without aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Abu Hanifa's position that a harbi captured without aman becomes fay' from the Sahibayn's position that the captor owns him exclusively, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding his release or conversion.

Core Idea
A harbi entering Dar al-Islam without aman is community property (fay') per Abu Hanifa, but exclusive to his captor per Sahibayn—capture timing matters.

When a harbi (enemy combatant) enters Muslim territory without a guarantee of safety (aman) and is captured, a critical intra-Hanafi dispute arises: does the captor own him, or does he belong to the entire community? This lesson explores the views of Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with special cases involving the Haram of Makkah and conversion before capture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
What is the legal status of a harbi who enters Dar al-Islam without aman and is captured by a Muslim? Is he owned by the captor or does he belong to the Muslim community as a whole?
2
State Abu Hanifa's Ruling
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the harbi does not become the exclusive property of the captor. Instead, he becomes fay'—booty that belongs to the entire Muslim community, similar to property acquired without a military expedition.
Fay' is distinct from ghanimah; it is not distributed among soldiers but goes to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal).
3
State the Sahibayn's Ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the captor acquires exclusive ownership of the harbi. This is the 'zahir qawluhuma' (apparent view of the two).
The Sahibayn analogize this to ghanimah, where a combatant's capture gives ownership to the individual soldier.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
If the captor releases the harbi, under Abu Hanifa the release is invalid unless the community agrees or the imam orders it; under Sahibayn the captor may free him as his own property. Similarly, if the harbi converts to Islam before capture, Sahibayn consider him free, whereas Abu Hanifa still treats him as fay'.
The text also mentions the special case of the Haram: Abu Hanifa allows capture inside the Haram, while Sahibayn forbid capture but forbid harming or expelling him.
5
Consider the Effect of Return to Dar al-Harb
If the harbi returns to Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) after entering without aman, he ceases to be fay' and regains freedom. This is agreed upon by all.
This shows that the fay' status is contingent on his presence in Dar al-Islam under capture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن دخل دار الإسلام بلا أمان فأخذه واحد من المسلمين لا يختص به عند أبي حنيفة بل يكون فيئاً لجماعة المسلمين، وظاهر قولهما أنه يختص به ولو دخل الحرم قبل أن يؤخذ فعند أبي حنيفة يؤخذ ويكون فيئاً للمسلمين، وعلى قولهما لا ولكن لا يطعم ولا يسقى ولا يؤذى ولا يخرج. وفي المحيط : إذا دخل دارنا بلا أمان فهو فيء عند الإمام أخذ قبل الإسلام أو بعده، وعندهما إن أسلم قبل الأخذ فهو حر ولو رجع هذا الحربي إلى دار الحرب خرج من أن يكون فيئاً وعاد حراً.
And if he enters Dar al-Islam without aman and one of the Muslims captures him, he is not exclusively for him according to Abu Hanifa, but rather becomes fay' for the entire Muslim community. The apparent view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that he is exclusive to the captor. And if he enters the Haram before being captured, according to Abu Hanifa he is captured and becomes fay' for the Muslims, but according to them he is not captured, yet he is not fed, given water, harmed, or expelled. In al-Muhit: If he enters our abode without aman, it is fay' according to the Imam whether captured before or after embracing Islam. According to them, if he embraces Islam before capture, he is free. And if this harbi returns to Dar al-Harb, he ceases to be fay' and becomes free again.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Abu Hanifa and his two major disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue: when a harbi (enemy combatant) enters Dar al-Islam without a guarantee of safety (aman) and is taken by a Muslim, who owns him? Abu Hanifa rules that such a person becomes fay'—booty belonging to the entire community, not the individual captor. The Sahibayn hold that the captor acquires exclusive ownership. A special case: if the harbi enters the sacred precinct of Makkah (al-Haram) before capture, Abu Hanifa still allows capture, making him fay', while the Sahibayn forbid capture but also forbid harming or expelling him—he is left unattended. Further details: if the harbi converts to Islam before capture, the Sahibayn consider him free; Abu Hanifa still considers him fay'. If he returns to Dar al-Harb, he ceases to be fay' and regains freedom.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Property taken from enemies without military force (or with less fighting), belonging to the entire Muslim community.
الأخذ (al-akhdh): Physical capture or seizure of a harbi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of a harbi who enters Dar al-Islam without aman and is captured?
A) He belongs exclusively to the captor
B) He becomes fay' for all Muslims
C) He is free
D) He must be taken to the imam

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a harbi entering without aman does not automatically become individual property; Abu Hanifa rules it as fay', while the Sahibayn grant ownership to the captor—a key intra-madhab difference.
